Types of Simple Words: Juz'i, Mutawatir, Mushakkak
Author classifies simple words by meaning: particular, univocal, ambiguous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any simple word as juz'i, mutawatir, or mushakkak based on the nature of its meaning and the equality of its individuals.

Core Idea
Simple words with one meaning are either particular, univocal, or graded.

In the previous lesson, we distinguished simple words from compound. Now we look deeper into simple words that have a single meaning. The author classifies them based on whether the meaning is particular to one individual or universal, and if universal, whether the individuals are equal in applying the word.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the word has one meaning
Focus on simple words whose meaning is one. If a word has multiple meanings (mushtarak), it is treated separately.
2
Check if the meaning is particular (juz'i)
If the word denotes a specific individual, it is juz'i. Examples: pronouns like 'ana' (I) and demonstratives like 'hadha' (this). Note that the coinage may be general, but the referent is specific.
This does not mean the word itself is particular; it means it is used for particulars.
3
If universal, test equality among individuals
If the word applies to multiple individuals, check if they are all equal in truth (mutawatir) or not (mushakkak). For mutawatir, e.g., 'insan' applies equally to all humans.
4
Understand mushakkak gradations
For mushakkak, the difference is in priority, worthiness, intensity, or increase. This is not in the essence of the thing itself but in the degree to which individuals possess the attribute. E.g., 'nur' (light) applies more intensely to the sun than to a lamp.
The mind can extract weaker instances from a stronger one.
5
Apply to examples from Islamic sciences
Practice with terms like 'wujud' (existence) – mushakkak between God and creatures; 'iman' (faith) – mushakkak among believers; 'sawad' (blackness) – not mushakkak, but 'aswad' is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَيْضًا إِنِ اتَّحَدَ مَعْنَاهُ، فَمَعَ تَشَخُصِهِ جُزْنِي، وَيَدْخُلُ فِيهِ الْمُضْمَرَاتُ وَأَسْمَاءُ الْإِشَارَاتِ ؛ فَإِنَّ الْوَضْعَ فِيهَا وَإِنْ كَانَ عَامَّا ، لَكِنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ لَهُ خَايٌّ ، عَلَى مَا هُوَ التَّحْقِيقُ ، وَبِدُونِهِ مُتَوَاطٍ، إِنْ تَسَاوَتْ أَقْرَادُهُ فِي الصِّدْقِ، وَإِلَّا فَمُشَكَّكَ، وَحَصَرُوا التَّفَاوُتَ فِي الْأَوَّلِيَّةِ وَالْأَوْلَوِيَّةِ وَالشَّدَّةِ وَالزَّيَادَةِ، وَلَا في الْمَاهِيَّاتِ وَلَا فِي الْعَوَارِضِ ، بَلْ فِي اتَّصَافِ الْأَفْرَادِ بِهَا، فَلَا تشكيك في الجِسْم وَلَا فِي السَّوَادِ، بَلْ في أَسْوَدَ، وَمَعْنِيَ كَوْنِ أَحَدِ الْفَرْدَيْنِ أَشَدَّ أَنَّهُ بِحَيْثُ يَنْتَزِعُ مِنْهُ الْعَقْلُ بِمَعُونَةِ الْوَهُم أَمْثَالَ الْأَضْعَفِ، وَيُحَلَّلُهُ إِلَيْهَا ، حَتى إِنَّ الْأَوْهَامَ الْعَامَّةَ تَذْهَبُ إِلَى أَنَّهُ مُتَأَلَفٌ مِنْهَا ، فَافْهَمْ.
Also, if its meaning is one, then with its particularity it is [denoting] a particular (juz'i), and it includes pronouns and demonstrative nouns, because the coinage in them is general, but the object for which they are coined is specific, according to the verification. And without that [particularity], it is univocal (mutawatir) if its individuals are equal in truthfulness, otherwise it is ambiguous (mushakkak). And they limited the difference to priority, worthiness, intensity, and increase, not in the essences nor in the accidents, but in the attribution of individuals to them. So there is no tashkik in 'body' or 'blackness', but in 'black'. And the meaning of one of the two individuals being more intense is that the mind, with the help of imagination, extracts from it instances of the weaker and analyzes it into them, to the point that common imaginations believe it is composed of them, so understand.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage classifies simple words that have one meaning. If the meaning is a particular (like a specific person), the word is called juz'i; this includes pronouns and demonstratives because although the coinage is general (e.g., 'ana' is coined for any speaker), the intended referent is always specific. If the meaning is universal and all individuals apply to it equally, it is mutawatir (univocal), e.g., 'insan' applies to all humans equally. If the individuals apply with varying degrees (like priority, intensity, etc.), it is mushakkak (ambiguous), e.g., 'wujud' (existence) applies to God with priority and to creatures secondarily. The author clarifies that the variation is not in the essence of the thing but in the degree to which individuals possess it. For example, 'aswad' (black) is mushakkak because some blacks are more intense, but 'sawad' (blackness) is not. He explains that the stronger individual is such that the mind can extract weaker instances from it and analyze it as if composed of them.

Key Terms
جزئي (juz'i): A word that denotes a particular individual.
متواطئ (mutawatir): A word that applies equally to all its individuals.
مشكك (mushakkak): A word that applies to its individuals with varying degrees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, where does the difference (tafawut) in a mushakkak term occur?
A) In the essence (mahiyya) of the thing
B) In the attribution of individuals to the property
C) In the accidents (awarid) of the thing
D) In both the essence and the accidents

Key Takeaway
The three types of simple words are distinguished by whether the meaning is particular or universal, and if universal, whether individuals apply equally or unequally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following Islamic terms as juz'i, mutawatir, or mushakkak: 'al-rahman' (the Merciful – a proper name for God? It is juz'i as a particular name), 'al-nur' (light – mushakkak, as in the sun vs a lamp), 'al-kitab' (book – mutawatir if all books are equally books? Actually it can be mushakkak if considering the Qur'an as the ultimate book). Provide reasoning.
?
Explain why 'sawad' (blackness) is not subject to tashkik but 'aswad' (black) is, using the author's distinction between the essence/attribute and its attribution to individuals.

The Five Universals
beginner
5 min
Concept (Mafhum): Universal and Particular
Author defines mafhum and divides it into kulli and juz'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) concepts based on the criterion of mental multiplicity, and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Universality depends on the mind's permission of multiplicity, not on external existence.

This opening passage establishes the foundational division of concepts into universal and particular. The criterion is purely mental: if the mind can conceive of multiple individuals under a concept, it is universal; otherwise, it is particular. This distinction is the key to understanding the five universals and the logic of definition in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mafhum
The author begins by referring to المفهوم (the conceptual content) and immediately applies a conditional division. Note that mafhum is the mental content of a term, not the external object.
This is the starting point for all logical analysis of terms.
2
Apply the criterion for universality
Read the condition: 'if the mind permits its multiplicity from the aspect of its conception, then it is universal'. Focus on the phrase 'from the aspect of its conception' — it is a mental permission, not a physical fact.
Even impossible concepts like 'partner of God' are universals because the mind can conceive of them applying to multiple individuals (even if none exist).
3
Examine the types of universals
The author mentions universals with impossible individuals (like hypothetical universals) and those with existent or non-existent individuals (like the Necessary and the contingent). This shows that actual existence is irrelevant.
Memorize these categories as examples of universal concepts.
4
Identify particulars
If the mind does not permit multiplicity, the concept is particular. The author gives three examples: a specific newborn child, a specific weak-sighted old man, and a mental image from a specific egg. These are unique in conception.
Each of these is inherently singular; you cannot predicate them of more than one.
5
Contrast with common misunderstanding
Many students mistakenly think 'universal' means having many real instances. The passage clarifies that it is a mental property. A universal can have zero actual individuals and still be universal.
This is a common pitfall; the passage explicitly includes 'impossible individuals' to prevent this error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَفْهُومُ إِنْ جَوَّزَ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُّرَهُ، مِنْ حَيْثُ تَصَوُّرُهُ، فَكُلِّيٌّ؛ مُمْتَنِعُ الْأَفْرَادِ كَالْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ، أَوْ لَا، إِمَّا مَعْدُومٌ أَوْ مَوْجُودٌ، كَالْوَاجِبِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ، وَإِلَّا فَجُزْئِيٌّ، فَمَحْبُوسُ الطِّفْلِ فِي مَبْدَإِ الْوِلَادَةِ، وَشَيْخٌ ضَعِيفُ الْبَصَرِ، وَالصُّورَةُ الْخَيَالِيَّةُ مِنَ الْبَيْضَةِ الْمُعَيَّنَةِ كُلُّهَا جُزْئِيَّاتٌ، لِأَنَّ شَيْئًا مِنْهَا لَا يُجَوِّزُ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُّرَهُ.
The concept (mafhūm): if the mind permits its multiplicity, from the aspect of its conception, then it is universal (kullī); [its individuals are] impossible [like the] hypothetical universals, or not, either non-existent or existent, like the Necessary and the contingent. Otherwise it is particular (juzʼī). So a child confined at the beginning of birth, an old man with weak eyesight, and the imaginary form from a specific egg — all these are particulars, because the mind does not permit multiplicity of any of them.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage defines المفهوم (the conceptual content) and divides it into two types:كلي (universal) and جزئي (particular). A concept is universal if the mind can conceive of multiple individuals falling under it. This includes universals that have no actual individuals (معدومة), like impossible entities (e.g., a partner of God), and those that are existent (موجودة), like the Necessary Being (واجب) and contingent beings (ممكن). If the mind cannot conceive of multiple individuals (i.e., the concept is unique by nature), it is particular. The author gives three examples: (1) a newborn infant (strictly speaking, a particular child), (2) a specific old man with weak sight (a particular person), (3) the mental image of a specific egg (a particular image in the mind). All these are particulars because the mind cannot apply them to more than one individual — they are inherently singular. This classification is foundational for understanding the five universals (الكليات الخمس) and definition in logic.

Key Terms
مَفْهُوم
كُلِّيّ
جُزْئِيّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes a concept universal according to this passage?
A) It has more than one individual existing in reality.
B) The mind allows it to have multiple individuals from the aspect of its conception.
C) It is predicated of all its individuals equally.
D) It is an abstract idea without any individuals.

Key Takeaway
Universality is a mental property: a concept is universal if the mind can conceive of more than one individual falling under it, regardless of actual existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the criterion from the passage, determine whether the concept 'divine wisdom' (al-ḥikma al-ilāhiyya) is universal or particular. Explain your reasoning.
?
Identify one example of a universal concept and one example of a particular concept from Islamic theology (kalam) that are not mentioned in the passage.

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Universal and Particular: Core Definitions and the Mental Image Doubt
Definition of universal and particular with doubt resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal and particular concepts and explain why the mental image of a particular remains particular despite multiple instances in different minds.

Core Idea
A particular has one external identity; its mental copies cannot multiply it.

The distinction between universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) is foundational in Islamic logic. A concept is universal if the mind can conceive it applying to multiple individuals; otherwise it is particular. But what about the mental image of Zayd that appears in many minds? The commentator resolves this famous doubt by showing that all those mental images share the same external identity (huwiyya), so the concept remains particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of universal and particular
Read the opening lines: 'The concept: if the mind allows its multiplication, from the perspective of its conception, then it is universal... Otherwise it is particular.' Note the key criterion: whether the mind can conceive multiple instances collectively.
The author also mentions hypothetical universals (no existing individuals) and particulars like a specific child or egg.
2
Analyze the famous doubt about mental images
The matn raises a doubt: the external image of Zayd is a particular, but mental images in different minds seem to be multiple instances. Why isn't that image a universal?
Consider the objection: if the same image appears in many minds, does it become universal?
3
Understand the commentator's resolution
The commentator argues that things exist in the mind 'by themselves,' not by copies. Therefore, the image in multiple minds is a single external identity (huwiyya) repeated, not multiplied externally. This preserves its particularity.
Also note the distinction: hypothetical universals lack identity, so the mind allows their multiplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَفْهُومُ إِنْ جَوَّزَ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُرَهُ ، مِنْ حَيْثُ تَصَوُّرُهُ، فَكُلِّيّ، مُمْتَنِعُ أَفْرَادُهُ ، كَالْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ ، أَوْ لَا ، إِمَّا مَعْدُومٌ أَوْ مَوْجُودٌ ، كَالْوَاجِبِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ، وَإِلَّا فَجُزْئِي، فَمَحْبُوسُ الطِّفْلِ فِي مَبْدَ الْوِلَادَةِ، وَشَيْخ ضَعِيفِ الْبَصَرِ، وَالصُّورَةِ الْخَيَالِيَّةِ مِنَ الْبَيْضَةِ الْمُعَيَّنَةِ كُلُّهَا جُزْئِيَّاتٌ ، لأَنَّ شَيْئًا مِنْهَا لَا يُجَوِّزُ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُرَهُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الاجْتِمَاع ، وَهُوَ الْمُرَادُ.
متن سلم العلوم
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الصُّورَةَ الْخَارِجِيَّةَ لِزَيْدٍ ، وَالصُّورَةَ الْحَاصِلَةَ مِنْهُ فِي أَذْهَانِ طَائِفَةٍ تَصَوَّرُوهَا كُلُّهَا مُتَصَادِقَةٌ ؛ فَإِنَّ التَّحْقِيقَ أَنَّ حُصُولَ الْأَشْيَاءِ بِأَنْفُسِهَا فِي الذَّهْنِ ، لَا بِأَشْبَاحِهَا وَأَمْثَالِهَا ، فَتِلْكَ الصُّورَةُ مُتَكَفِّرَةٌ، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَتَبَيَّنُ كَوْنُ الْجُزْئِيِّ الْحَقِيقِيِّ مَحْمُولا ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ .
وَلَا يُجَابُ بِأَنَّ الْمُرَادَ صِدْقُهَا عَلَى الكَثِيرِينَ هُوَ ظِلٌّ لَهَا وَمُنْتَزَعُ عَنْهَا، وَاللَّازِمُ هَهُنَا أَنَّ لَهَا ظِلَّا مُتَعَدِّدًا ، لَا أَنَّهَا ظِلّ مُتَعَدِّدٌ، وَالْمَطْلُوبُ هُوَ الثَّاني ؛ وَذَلِكَ لأَنَّ التَّصَادُقَ يُصَحْحُ الاِنْتِرَاعَ وَالظُّليَّةَ أَيْضًا ، فَإِنَّ الاِتِّحَادَ مِنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ. ؛ بَلِ الْجَوَابُ أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ تَكَثُرُ الْمَفْهُومِ بِحَسَبِ الْخَارِجِ، فَالصُّورَةُ الْحَاصِلَةُ مِنْ زَيْدِ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْأَذْهَانِ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ تَكَبَّرَ في الخارج، بَلْ كُلُّهَا هُويَّةُ زَيْدٍ. وَأَمَّا الكُليّاتُ الفَرْضِيَّةُ وَالْمَعْقُولَاتُ الثَّانِيَةُ فَلِعَدَمِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الْهَذِيَّةِ لَا يَنْقَبِضُ الْعَقْلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِهَا عَنْ تَجْوِيزِ تَكَثُرِهَا فِي الْخَارِج، حَتَّى قِيلَ: إِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الفَرْضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إلى الْحَقَائِقِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ كُلَّيَّاتٌ .
هَذَا، وَالْكُلَّيه وَالْجُزْنِيَّةُ صِفَةُ الْمَعْلُومِ، وَقِيلَ صِفَةُ الْعِلْمِ وَالْجُزْنِيُّ لَا يَكُونُ كَاسِبًا وَلَا مُكْتَسَبَا وَقَدْ يُقَالُ الْجُزْنِيُّ لِكُلِّ مُندَرجِ تَحْتَ كُلِّي أَعَمَّ، وَيَخْتَصُّ بِالْإِضَافِيِّ كَالْأَوَّلِ بِالْحَقِيقِيُّ.
The concept: if the mind allows its multiplication, from the perspective of its conception, then it is universal, [but] its individuals are prevented (like the hypothetical universals), or [if it does not allow multiplication] then [it is] either non-existent or existent, like the Necessary and the Possible. Otherwise it is particular. So the child confined from the beginning of birth, the weak-sighted old man, and the imaginal form of a specific egg are all particulars, because the mind does not permit their multiplication in terms of aggregation, and that is what is intended.
Matn of Sullam al-Ulum: And here there is a famous doubt, namely that the external image of Zayd and the image obtained from him in the minds of a group who conceive it are all co-extensive. For verification is that things obtain by themselves in the mind, not by their wraiths and likenesses, so that image is repeated. And from here it becomes clear that the true particular is predicable, and that is the truth.
It is not answered by saying that what is meant is that its truth of many is a shadow of it and abstracted from it, for the necessary here is that it has multiple shadows, not that it is multiple shadows, and the desired is the second. That is because co-extensiveness validates abstraction and shadow-ness as well, for the unity is from both sides. Rather the answer is that what is meant is the multiplication of the concept with respect to external reality; the image obtained from Zayd with respect to minds is impossible to multiply externally; rather all of it is the identity of Zayd. As for the hypothetical universals and the secondary intelligibles, because they do not include identity, the mind, merely by conceiving them, does not restrain from allowing their multiplication externally, so it is said: the hypothetical universals with respect to existing realities are universals.
This, and universality and particularity are attributes of the known; and it is said they are attributes of knowledge; and the particular is not an acquirer nor acquired; and it has been said the particular is everything subsumed under a universal in general, and it is restricted to the relative (idafi) like the first [i.e., true particular]? Actually the text says "وَيَخْتَصُّ بِالْإِضَافِيِّ كَالْأَوَّلِ بِالْحَقِيقِيُّ" which is unclear; likely means "and the particular is restricted to the relative like the first [type] is restricted to the true [particular]."
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage defines the universal (kulli) and the particular (juz'i). The author states that a concept is universal if the mind can conceive it applying to multiple individuals; otherwise it is particular. He gives examples of universals with no individuals (hypothetical universals) and universals that are existing (like the Necessary and the Possible). Particulars are things like a specific person or a specific mental image that the mind cannot apply to multiple distinct individuals collectively. Then the author quotes the matn of Sullam al-Ulum, which raises a famous doubt: the external image of Zayd is a particular, but the mental images of Zayd in different minds all correspond to the same external reality, so they seem to be co-extensive (mutasadiqa). How can they be particular if multiple minds have them? The commentator explains that the truth is that things exist by themselves in the mind, not by copies, so that image is repeated (mutakarrara) in different minds. This shows that the true particular can be predicated (of many) — i.e., it can be a predicate in a proposition. Then he rejects a potential objection that 'true of many' is just a shadow abstracted from the particular. He argues that co-extensiveness validates both abstraction and shadow, but the desired point is that the particular is not multiple shadows. Instead, the correct answer is that the multiplication is in terms of external reality: the image obtained from Zayd in multiple minds cannot multiply externally; all those images are one identity (huwiyya) of Zayd. Finally, he discusses hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles: they lack identity (huwiyya) so the mind does not restrict them from external multiplication. Then he adds that universality and particularity are attributes of the known (or of knowledge). The particular is not an acquirer nor acquired; it can be said that every particular is subsumed under a universal, and it is restricted to the relative (idafi) particular as opposed to the true (haqiqi) particular.

Key Terms
الْمَفْهُوم
الْكُلِّي
الْجُزْئِي
الْهُوِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the mental image of Zayd in different minds still a particular and not a universal?
A) Because the image is only in one mind at a time.
B) Because the image in each mind is a different image.
C) Because all those images share the same external identity (huwiyya) of Zayd, so they cannot multiply externally.
D) Because the mind cannot conceive of Zayd's image applying to multiple individuals.

Key Takeaway
The particular has a single external identity; its mental repetition does not make it universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept 'human'. Is it universal or particular? Justify using the definition from this lesson.
?
A student argues that the image of the Kaaba in two different minds is a universal because it applies to two instances. Using the commentator's resolution, explain why this is mistaken.

Four Relations Between Two Universals
Author classifies the four possible relations between two universals based on coextensiveness and inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any two universals into one of the four relations (equality, opposition, general-specific from one aspect, general-specific absolutely) and provide an Islamic example for each.

Core Idea
Four relations define how universals relate: complete overlap, complete exclusion, partial overlap, or full inclusion.

This passage presents the foundational classification of relations between two universals. It's the key to understanding how concepts in the Islamic sciences relate to each other—whether in definitions, legal categorisations, or theological attributes. By mastering this classification, you will be able to analyse the logical structure of terms used in fiqh, kalam, and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Begin by clearly stating the two universals under consideration, e.g., 'animal' and 'human' or 'believer' and 'Muslim'.
2
Check for complete coincidence
Do the two universals apply to exactly the same set of individuals? If yes, they are equal (mutasawiyan). If not, move to the next step.
Example: 'human' and 'rational animal' are co-extensive.
3
Check for complete exclusion
Do the two universals have no individuals in common? If both are universal and disjoint, they are opposed (mutabayinan). If not, move on.
Example: 'human' and 'horse' have no common members.
4
Determine the kind of general-specific relation
If one is particular (juz'i) relative to the other, then check: if each is particular from the other's side, it is general-specific from one aspect (a'am wa akhass min wajh). If only one side is particular, it is general-specific absolutely (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan).
Example of partial overlap: 'human' and 'white'. Example of full inclusion: 'animal' and 'human'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْكُلِّيَّانِ إِنْ تَصَادَقَا كُلَّيًّا فَمُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا تَفَارَقَا، فَإِنْ كَانَ كُلِّيًّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فَمُتَبَايِنَانِ ، وَإِنْ كَانَ جُزْيْبًا فَإِمَّا مِن الْجَانِبَيْنِ، فَأَعَمُ وَأَخَصُّ مِنْ وَجْهِ، أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبِ وَاحِدٍ فَقَطْ ، فَأَعَمُ وَأَخَصُ مُطلَقًا.
Two universals: if they coincide completely, they are equal (mutasawiyan); otherwise they differ. If [the difference] is that each is universal, then they are opposed (mutabayinan). If one is particular (juz'iyyan), then either it is particular from both sides, so [they are] general and specific from one aspect (a'am wa akhass min wajh), or it is particular from one side only, so [they are] general and specific absolutely (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the relations between two universals. The author states that if two universals are co-extensive (apply to exactly the same individuals), they are called equal (mutasawiyan), like 'human' and 'rational animal'. Otherwise, they differ. If both are universal and mutually exclusive (no common individuals), they are opposed (mutabayinan), like 'human' and 'horse'. If one is universal and the other particular (in the sense of being subsumed under the universal), then if each is particular relative to the other — meaning they overlap partially — they are said to be general and specific from one aspect (a'am wa akhass min wajh), like 'human' (which includes male and female) and 'white' (which includes non-humans). If one is purely general and the other purely specific, with one containing all individuals of the other and more, they are general and specific absolutely (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan), like 'animal' (general) and 'human' (specific). This classification is fundamental in logic for understanding the relationships between concepts.

Key Terms
مُتَسَاوِيَان (mutasawiyan) - co-extensive
مُتَبَايِنَان (mutabayinan) - disjoint
أَعَمُ وَأَخَصُّ مِنْ وَجْه (a'am wa akhass min wajh) - general and specific from one aspect
أَعَمُ وَأَخَصُ مُطْلَقًا (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan) - general and specific absolutely

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relation between 'animal' (hayawan) and 'human' (insan) according to this classification?
A) Equal (mutasawiyan)
B) Opposed (mutabayinan)
C) General and specific from one aspect (a'am wa akhass min wajh)
D) General and specific absolutely (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan)

Key Takeaway
The four relations between universals are fundamental to logical definition and conceptual analysis in the Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Understanding Universals: Hypothetical and Secondary Intelligibles
Author classifies hypothetical universals and defines universality/particularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish hypothetical universals from real universals and explain the attributes of universality and particularity as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Hypothetical universals are mental constructs that apply to multiple individuals without existing in reality.

Before we can use the five universals for definitions and syllogisms, we must understand what a universal is. The author begins by clarifying two types: hypothetical universals (al-kulliyyat al-fardiyya) and secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). These differ from real universals because they lack individual essence (huwiyya). This lesson unpacks these distinctions and explains the attributes of universality and particularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify hypothetical universals
Locate the term 'al-kulliyyat al-fardiyya' and note that they are universals that have no external instance. The passage says the mind does not 'shrink' from conceiving them as multiple externally because they lack huwiyya (individual essence).
Example: 'impossible' is a hypothetical universal—nothing in existence instantiates it, but it applies to many concepts.
2
Understand the reason for mental multiplicity
The mind permits hypothetical universals to be many because they do not include huwiyya. Contrast this with real universals which are instantiated in individuals.
This distinction is important for understanding how secondary intelligibles (like 'universality' itself) are predicated.
3
Examine the attributes of universality and particularity
The passage states these are attributes of the known (al-ma'lum) or of knowledge. Understand that universality is not a property of things in the world but of concepts in the mind.
The debate: is 'universal' a property of the object or the knowledge? The author presents both views.
4
Clarify the specific and general
The passage defines 'particular' as that which falls under a more general universal. It also distinguishes between relative particular (al-idafi) and true particular (al-haqiqi).
This paves the way for understanding the four relations in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ وَالْمَعْقُولَاتُ الثَّانِيَةُ فَلِعَدَمِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الْهُوِيَّةِ لَا يَنْقَبِضُ الْعَقْلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِهَا عَنْ تَجْوِيزِ تَكَثُرِهَا فِي الْخَارِجِ، حَتَّى قِيلَ: إِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَقَائِقِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ كُلِّيَّاتٌ. هَذَا، وَالْكُلِّيَّةُ وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ صِفَةُ الْمَعْلُومِ، وَقِيلَ صِفَةُ الْعِلْمِ. وَالْجُزْئِيُّ لَا يَكُونُ كَاسِبًا وَلَا مُكْتَسَبًا، وَقَدْ يُقَالُ الْجُزْئِيُّ لِكُلِّ مُنْدَرِجٍ تَحْتَ كُلِّيٍّ أَعَمَّ، وَيَخْتَصُّ بِالْإِضَافِيِّ كَالْأَوَّلِ بِالْحَقِيقِيِّ. الْكُلِّيَّانِ إِنْ تَصَادَقَا كُلِّيًّا فَمُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا تَفَارَقَا، فَإِنْ كَانَ كُلِّيًّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فَمُتَبَايِنَانِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ جُزْئِيًّا فَإِمَّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فَأَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مِنْ وَجْهٍ، أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبٍ وَاحِدٍ فَقَطْ فَأَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مُطْلَقًا.
As for the hypothetical universals and the secondary intelligibles, because they do not include the essence (huwiyya), the mind does not shrink from permitting their multiplicity in the external, such that it is said: the hypothetical universals are universals in relation to the existing realities. This, and universality and particularity are attributes of the known (al-ma'lum), and it is said they are attributes of knowledge. The particular is not an acquisitor nor acquired, and sometimes the particular is said for everything that falls under a more general universal, and it is specific to the relative (al-idafi) just as the first is for the true. The two universals: if they co-extend universally, they are equal; otherwise they diverge: if both sides are universal, they are disparate; if one is particular, then either from both sides — they are more general and more specific in one respect, or from one side only — they are more general and more specific absolutely.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage classifies types of universals and defines the four relations between them. First, the author discusses hypothetical universals (al-kulliyyat al-fardiyya) and secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). These differ from real universals because they lack individual essence (huwiyya); therefore the mind can conceive of them as multiple in the external. For example, abstract concepts like 'animality' can be predicated of many. Next, the author states that universality and particularity are attributes of the known (the object of knowledge) according to one view, or of knowledge itself. The particular is neither an acquisitor (kasib) nor acquired (muktasab) — this refers to a classification in the context of definition. The particular is said to be that which falls under a more general universal, and it is specified as relative (idafi) in contrast to the true particular (haqiqi). The core of the passage then enumerates the four relations between two universal terms: (1) equal (mutasawiyan) when they co-extend completely; (2) disparate (mutabayinan) when they have no common individuals; (3) more general and more specific in one respect (a'am wa akhass min wajh) when they partially overlap; (4) more general and more specific absolutely (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan) when one fully includes the other. These relationships are essential for constructing definitions and evaluating syllogisms.

Key Terms
الكليات الفرضية
المعقولات الثانوية
الكلية
الجزئية
الجزئي الإضافي
الجزئي الحقيقي

B) Because they lack huwiyya (individual essence)
C) Because they are attributes of knowledge
D) Because they are always instantiated

Key Takeaway
Hypothetical universals and secondary intelligibles are mental concepts that can be predicated of many without having external individuals, and universality/particularity are attributes of the known or of knowledge.

The Four Relations Between Universals
Author enumerates the four relations between two universal concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct examples for each of the four relations between two universal concepts: equal, disparate, partial overlap, and absolute inclusion.

Core Idea
The four relations between universals form the backbone of all definition and syllogism analysis in Islamic logic.

Now that we understand what universals are, we need to know how they relate to each other. This lesson unpacks the four logical relations that can hold between any two universal terms: equality, disparity, partial overlap (general and specific in one respect), and absolute inclusion (general and specific absolutely). Mastering these relations is essential for constructing valid definitions and evaluating syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four relation conditions
The passage gives a decision tree: first check if the two universals co-extend completely (equal). If not, they diverge. Then check if both sides are universal (disparate). If one is particular (i.e., a universal relative to another), then see if the overlap is from both sides (partial) or one side (absolute inclusion).
The 'particular' here means a universal that is more specific than the other, not an individual.
2
Understand 'equal' universals
Two universals are equal (mutasawiyan) if they have exactly the same extension. Example: 'human' and 'rational animal' have the same individuals.
In definitions, equal terms can be substituted.
3
Differentiate 'disparate' from other relations
Disparate (mutabayinan) means no common individuals. Both universals are universal. Example: 'human' and 'stone' have no overlap.
This is important for syllogisms: no middle term can connect two disparates directly.
4
Distinguish partial overlap from absolute inclusion
Partial overlap (a'am wa akhass min wajh) occurs when each universal shares some individuals with the other but neither fully includes the other. Example: 'human' and 'white' overlap in white humans, but not all humans are white, and not all white things are human. Absolute inclusion (a'am wa akhass mutlaqan) occurs when one fully contains the other. Example: 'animal' includes all 'humans'.
The 'particular' side in absolute inclusion is the more specific universal; the other is the more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ وَالْمَعْقُولَاتُ الثَّانِيَةُ فَلِعَدَمِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الْهُوِيَّةِ لَا يَنْقَبِضُ الْعَقْلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِهَا عَنْ تَجْوِيزِ تَكَثُرِهَا فِي الْخَارِجِ، حَتَّى قِيلَ: إِنَّ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَقَائِقِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ كُلِّيَّاتٌ. هَذَا، وَالْكُلِّيَّةُ وَالْجُزْئِيَّةُ صِفَةُ الْمَعْلُومِ، وَقِيلَ صِفَةُ الْعِلْمِ. وَالْجُزْئِيُّ لَا يَكُونُ كَاسِبًا وَلَا مُكْتَسَبًا، وَقَدْ يُقَالُ الْجُزْئِيُّ لِكُلِّ مُنْدَرِجٍ تَحْتَ كُلِّيٍّ أَعَمَّ، وَيَخْتَصُّ بِالْإِضَافِيِّ كَالْأَوَّلِ بِالْحَقِيقِيِّ. الْكُلِّيَّانِ إِنْ تَصَادَقَا كُلِّيًّا فَمُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِلَّا تَفَارَقَا، فَإِنْ كَانَ كُلِّيًّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فَمُتَبَايِنَانِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ جُزْئِيًّا فَإِمَّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فَأَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مِنْ وَجْهٍ، أَوْ مِنْ جَانِبٍ وَاحِدٍ فَقَطْ فَأَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مُطْلَقًا.

Essential vs Accidental Universals
Author classifies universals into essential and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given universal as essential or accidental based on whether it is inside or outside the essence of the subject.

Core Idea
Essential universals are part of the definition; accidental are external.

The author here divides all universals into two broad categories: those that are essential (dhātī) and those that are accidental (ʿaraḍī). This classification is foundational for understanding how definitions and descriptions work in Islamic logic. Let's walk through the criteria the author provides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal in question
Determine the term that is being considered as a universal predicate. For example, 'animal', 'rational', 'white'.
The universal must be a predicate applicable to many subjects.
2
Check if it is identical to the reality or internal
If the universal is the very reality of the individuals ('ayn al-ḥaqīqa) or inside it (dākhil), it is essential. For example, 'human' is identical, 'animal' is internal.
Internal universals can be fully shared (genus) or not shared (differentia).
3
If external, classify as accidental
If the universal is outside the essence (khārij), it is accidental, whether it is proper to one species (e.g., 'risible') or common to many (e.g., 'white').
Accidental universals do not enter the definition.
4
Apply the test of conceivability
Can the subject be conceived without this universal? If not, it is essential; if yes, accidental. For example, you cannot conceive a human without rationality, but you can without whiteness.
This is a practical tool derived from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْكُلّيُّ إِمَّا عَيْنُ حَقِيقَةِ الْأَفْرَادِ ، أَوْ دَاخِل فِيهَا ، تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعِ آخَرَ ، أَوْ لَا ، وَيُقَالُ لَهَا ذَاتِيَّاتٌ، وَرُبَّمَا يُطْلَقُ الذَّاتِيُّ بِمَعْنَى الدَّاخِلِ. أَوْ خَارِجٌ ، مُخْتَصُّ بِحَقِيقَةٍ ، أَوْ لَا ، وَيُقَالُ لَهُمَا عَرَضيَّاتٌ . وَالْجُمْهُورُ عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَرَضَ غَيْرُ الْعَرَضِيٌّ وَغَيْرُ الْمَحَل حَقِيقَةٌ. قَالَ بَعْضُ الْأَفَاضِل مِنَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ: طَبِيعَةُ الْعَرَضِ لَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ عَرَضِيٌّ ، وَبِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ الْمَحَلُّ ، وَبِشَرْطِ لَا شَيْءٍ الْعَرَضُ الْمُقَابِلُ لِلْجَوْهَرِ . وَلِذَا صَحَ النِّسْوَةُ أَرْبَعٌ ، وَالْمَاءُ ذِرَاعٌ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ هَذَا الْمُشْتَقُ لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ، وَلَا عَلَى الْمَوْصُوفِ ، لَا عَامَّا وَلَا خَاصَّا، بَلْ مَعْنَاهُ هُوَ الْقَدْرُ النَّاعِتُ وَحْدَهُ، هَذَا هُوَ الْحَقُّ ، وَيُؤَيَّدُهُ مَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِينَا وُجُودُ الْأَعْرَاضِ فِي أَنْفُسِهَا هُوَ وُجُودُهَا لِمَحَالَهَا .
Then the universal: either it is the very reality of the individuals, or inside it, fully shared between it and another type, or not; and these are called essential (dhātiyyāt). And sometimes dhātī is used to mean 'that which is inside'. Or [it is] outside, specific to one reality, or not; and these two are called accidental ('araḍiyyāt). And the majority hold that 'araḍ (accident) is other than 'araḍī (accidental) and other than the subject (maḥall) in reality. Some of the distinguished investigators said: The nature of 'araḍ without any condition is 'araḍī; with the condition of something it is the subject (maḥall); with the condition of nothing it is the accident as opposed to substance. Thus it is correct that 'the woman is four [cubits]' and 'the water is a cubit'. And from this, this derived term (al-mushtaqq) does not indicate the relation, nor the described object, neither generally nor specifically; rather its meaning is solely the measure of description. This is the truth. And it is supported by what Ibn Sīnā said: 'The existence of accidents in themselves is their existence for their subjects.'
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of universals. It begins by dividing the universal into two broad categories: essential (dhāti) and accidental ('araḍī). Essential universals are either identical to the reality (ʿayn al-ḥaqīqa) of the individuals (i.e., species itself), or internal (dākhil) to it, meaning they are part of the essence. If they are internal, they may be fully shared between the individuals and another type (which is genus, e.g., 'animal' is shared between human and horse), or not shared (which is differentia, e.g., 'rational'). These are called essential (dhātiyyāt). The author notes that sometimes 'dhātī' is used to mean simply 'internal' (dākhil). On the other hand, accidental universals are external (khārij) to the essence. They may be specific to one reality (like 'risibility' for humans) or common to multiple realities (like 'whiteness' for many things). Both types are called accidental ('araḍiyyāt). The author then distinguishes between 'araḍ (accident in the sense of a property that inheres in a substance) and 'araḍī (the universal accidental). The majority hold that they are different, and also that the accident is distinct from its subject (maḥall) in reality. A refined position from some investigators (muḥaqqiqīn) explains: The nature of accident considered without any condition is the accidental universal ('araḍī); considered with the condition of being in a subject, it is the subject (maḥall) itself? Actually the text says 'with the condition of something it is the subject' — meaning when the accident is considered as inhering in a subject, the subject is included in the concept? This is clarified by the examples: 'the woman is four cubits' – here 'four cubits' is an accident (height) attributed to the woman; the phrase 'the water is a cubit' similarly. The author then makes a point about derived terms (mushtaqq): they do not indicate the relation (nisba) nor the described object (mawṣūf); rather they indicate only the measure (qadr) or the description itself (nāʿit). For example, 'white' does not mean 'something that has whiteness' but rather just 'whiteness itself'? This is a nuanced position. The passage concludes by citing Ibn Sina that the existence of accidents in themselves is their existence for their subjects, i.e., their being is their being-in-another.

Key Terms
كلي
ذاتي
عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered an essential universal?
A) Whiteness, because it is predicated of many things.
B) Rationality, because it is internal to the reality of a human.
C) Risible, because it is a property unique to humans.
D) Existence, because it is common to all things.

Key Takeaway
Essential universals are those that enter into the definition of the subject, either as genus, species, or differentia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following universals as essential or accidental for 'human': 'animal', 'rational', 'white', 'risible'. Use the criteria from the passage.

Distinguishing 'Arad from 'Aradī and the Nature of Derived Terms
Author distinguishes 'arad from 'aradī and discusses derived terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between the ontological accident ('arad) and the accidental universal ('aradī), and how this distinction bears on the interpretation of derived terms like 'white'.

Core Idea
'Arad is being-in-a-subject; 'aradī is a universal outside the essence — they are not the same.

After classifying universals, the author clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction: the term 'arad (accident in ontology) is not the same as 'aradī (accidental universal). Furthermore, he discusses a refined view from some investigators about how to understand these terms, and connects it to the semantics of derived terms (mushtaqq). This lesson unpacks that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct concepts
The passage states that the majority hold 'arad (ontological accident) is other than 'aradī (accidental universal). 'Arad is a property that inheres in a substance; 'aradī is a universal that is external to the essence of its subject.
For example, whiteness as an accident in a white object is 'arad; the concept 'white' as a predicate is 'aradī.
2
Understand the threefold distinction with conditions
Some investigators analyze the nature of accident: without any condition, it is 'aradī; with the condition of something (i.e., being in a subject), it is the subject itself (maḥall); with the condition of nothing, it is the accident as opposed to substance.
This shows that 'arad and 'aradī are aspects of the same nature viewed differently.
3
Apply to examples
The examples 'the woman is four cubits' and 'the water is a cubit' illustrate that the accident (height) is predicated of the subject, but the term 'four cubits' is not an accidental universal; it is a measure of description.
These show how accidents are predicated without implying they are universals.
4
Connect to derived terms
The author concludes that derived terms (mushtaqq) like 'tall' indicate only the measure of description (al-qadr al-nāʿit), not the relation or the described object. This aligns with Ibn Sina's view that accidents exist only for their subjects.
This refines our understanding of how predicates work in propositions.

Genus: The First Universal and Its Two Levels
Author defines genus and distinguishes close and distant genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish close genus from distant genus and identify which one answers about the complete essence.

Core Idea
Genus is the universal that answers 'what is it?' — close genus reveals the full essence, distant genus only a part.

In this lesson, we study the first of the five universals: genus (jins). The author defines it as a universal predicated of many differing in essence when answering 'what is it?'. Then he distinguishes two types: close genus (qarīb) which answers about the complete essence, and distant genus (ba'īd) which does not. This distinction is crucial for constructing correct definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'al-jins' in the Arabic passage. Notice the definition structure: 'kulliyun maqoolun 'ala katheerin mukhtalifeen bil-haqaaiq fi jawaabi ma huwa.' This is the core definition.
The definition includes three conditions: universal, predicated of many, differing in realities, in answer to 'what is it?'.
2
Understand the condition 'in answer to what is it?'
Genus is not just any universal; it specifically answers the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?). This distinguishes it from accident, which answers 'ayyu shay'in huwa' (what kind is it?).
3
Distinguish close and distant genus
The passage gives a criterion: if the genus answers about the complete essence (al-mahiyya) and all the participants (jamee' al-musharikaat), it is close (qarīb); otherwise, it is distant (ba'īd).
Close genus is immediate; distant genus is higher in the hierarchy (e.g., 'substance' is distant for humans).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the genus 'animal' (hayawān) for humans and horses. Since 'animal' answers about the complete essence of both and includes all participants (humans, horses, etc.), it is a close genus. If we go higher to 'body' (jism), it is distant because it does not fully specify the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالْكُلِّيَاتُ خَمْسٌ ، الْأَوَّلُ: الْجِنْسُ ، وَهُوَ: كُلّي، مَقُولٌ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ بِالْحَقَائِقِ، فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، فَإِنْ كَانَ جَوَابًا عَنْ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَجَمِيعِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ فَقَرِيبٌ ، وَإِلَّا فبعيد. وَهَهُنَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ مَا هُوَ سُؤَالٌ عَنْ تَمَامِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ إِنِ اقْتُصِرَ فِيهِ عَلَى أَمْرٍ وَاحِدٍ ، فَيُجَابُ بِالنَّوْعِ ، أَوْ بِالْحَدِّ التَّامَ، وَعَنْ تَمَامِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ
Thus the universals are five. The first: genus, and it is: a universal said of many differing in realities, in answer to 'what is it?'. If it answers about the essence and all the participants, then it is close; otherwise it is distant. And here are inquiries. The first: that 'what is it?' is a question about the complete specific essence. If it is confined to one matter, then it is answered by the species or by the complete definition. And about the complete essence...
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage begins the enumeration of the five universals, starting with genus (jins). The author defines genus as a universal that is predicated of many things that differ in essence (ḥaqā'iq) when they are asked 'what is it?' (mā huwa). For example, when asked 'What is a human?' and 'What is a horse?', the answer 'animal' applies to both, even though they differ in reality. Then the author distinguishes between close genus (qarīb) and distant genus (ba'īd). A close genus is one that answers about the complete essence (māhiyyah) and includes all the participants fully, meaning it is the immediate genus that directly includes the species. A distant genus is one that is further up the hierarchy, like 'substance' for humans. The criterion is: if it answers about the essence and all the things that share in it (i.e., all the species under that genus), it is close; otherwise it is distant. For instance, 'animal' is a close genus for humans and horses; 'body' is more distant. The passage then begins a discussion about the nature of the question 'what is it?'. It says that 'what is it?' is a question about the complete essence of the thing (tamām al-māhiyya al-mukhtaṣṣa). If the question is confined to one aspect (amr wāḥid), the answer is either the species (if the essence is simple) or the complete definition (ḥadd tāmm) if the essence is composite. Unfortunately, the text cuts off here, but it is clear that the author is setting up a discussion on how to answer the question 'what is it?' using the five universals. This passage is foundational for understanding the first universal, genus, which is a key component in definition and classification in Islamic logic.

بعيد
ما هو

D) A close genus is used in definition; a distant genus is used in demonstration.

Key Takeaway
Genus is a universal that answers 'what is it?'; close genus reveals the complete essence, while distant genus does not.

Classifying the Universal: Essential and Accidental
Author classifies the universal into essential and accidental and clarifies 'arad, 'aradi, and mahal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three categories of the universal and correctly differentiate between 'arad, 'aradi, and mahal.

Core Idea
The universal is either essential or accidental, but 'aradi is not the same as 'arad.

This passage from Bahr al-Ulum lays out a foundational classification of universals and clarifies a terminological confusion. You will learn the tripartite division of the universal and the precise meanings of three related terms that often cause confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of the universal
Find in the Arabic where the author says 'Then the universal: either it is the very reality of the individuals, or internal to them... or external...'. That gives three categories: identical to the individuals (the species itself), internal (essential), or external (accidental).
The first category is the species as a whole; the second includes genus and differentia; the third includes accidents.
2
Understand the essential/accidental distinction
Notice that 'internal' parts are called essential (dhatiyyat) and 'external' parts are called accidental (aradiyyat). Essential means part of the quiddity; accidental means external to it.
Sometimes 'essential' is used narrowly to mean internal only.
3
Distinguish 'arad, 'aradi, and mahal
The author explains that the nature of the accident (tabi'at al-'arad) when taken unconditioned (la bi-shart shay') is 'aradi; when conditioned by a subject (bi-shart shay') is al-mahal; when conditioned by nothing (bi-shart la shay') is 'arad opposite to substance.
So 'aradi is the universal accident concept, 'arad is the metaphysical category, and mahal is the subject bearing it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاللَّا حَيَوَانِ، وَالْإِنْسَانِ وَاللَّانَاطِقِ، وَقَدْ يَتَحَقَّقُ فِي ضِمْنِ الْعُمُومِ مِنْ وَجْهِ، كَالْأَبْيَض وَالْإِنْسَانِ، وَالْحَجَرِ وَالْحَيَوَانِ. وَهَهُنَا سُوَالٌ وَجَوَابٌ عَلَى طِبْقِ مَا مَرَّ. ثُمَّ الْكُلّيُّ إِمَّا عَيْنُ حَقِيقَةِ الْأَفْرَادِ ، أَوْ دَاخِل فِيهَا ، تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَ نَوْعِ آخَرَ ، أَوْ لَا ، وَيُقَالُ لَهَا ذَاتِيَّاتٌ، وَرُبَّمَا يُطْلَقُ الذَّاتِيُّ بِمَعْنَى الدَّاخِلِ. أَوْ خَارِجٌ ، مُخْتَصُّ بِحَقِيقَةٍ ، أَوْ لَا ، وَيُقَالُ لَهُمَا عَرَضيَّاتٌ . وَالْجُمْهُورُ عَلَى أَنَّ الْعَرَضَ غَيْرُ الْعَرَضِيٌّ وَغَيْرُ الْمَحَل حَقِيقَةٌ. قَالَ بَعْضُ الْأَفَاضِل مِنَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ: طَبِيعَةُ الْعَرَضِ لَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ عَرَضِيٌّ ، وَبِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ الْمَحَلُّ ، وَبِشَرْطِ لَا شَيْءٍ الْعَرَضُ الْمُقَابِلُ لِلْجَوْهَرِ . وَلِذَا صَحَ النِّسْوَةُ أَرْبَعٌ ، وَالْمَاءُ ذِرَاعٌ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ هَذَا الْمُشْتَقُ لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ، وَلَا عَلَى الْمَوْصُوفِ ، لَا عَامَّا وَلَا خَاصَّا، بَلْ مَعْنَاهُ هُوَ الْقَدْرُ النَّاعِتُ وَحْدَهُ، هَذَا هُوَ الْحَقُّ ، وَيُؤَيِّدُهُ مَا قَالَ ابْنُ سِينَا وُجُودُ الْأَعْرَاضِ فِي أَنْفُسِهَا هُوَ وُجُودُهَا لِمَحَالَهَا .
And the non-animal, and man and the non-rational, and it may be realized within generality from one respect, like the white and man, and the stone and the animal. And here is a question and answer in accordance with what has passed. Then the universal: either it is the very reality of the individuals, or internal to them, a complete common [part] between them and another species, or not, and they are called essential (dhatiyyat). And sometimes the essential is used in the sense of the internal. Or external, specific to a reality, or not, and they are called accidental (aradiyyat). And the majority hold that the accident (arad) is other than the accidental (aradi) and other than the subject (mahal) in reality. Some of the distinguished verifying scholars said: The nature of the accident, unconditioned by anything, is the accidental; conditioned by something, it is the subject; conditioned by nothing, it is the accident opposite to substance. And because of this, it is correct that the number is four, and the water is a cubit. And hence he said this derivative does not indicate the relation, nor the described, neither generally nor specifically, but its meaning is only the describing quantity alone. This is the truth. And it is supported by what Ibn Sina said: The existence of accidents in themselves is their existence for their subjects.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion about the universal. The author begins by giving examples of partial inclusion (like 'white' and 'man' share some subjects but not all, 'stone' and 'animal' are mutually exclusive?) and then states that there is a question and answer about what preceded. Then the main classification: The universal is either (1) identical to the reality of its individuals (like a species itself), (2) internal to them as a common part (like genus shared between species), or (3) external to them (like an accident). The internal and external are called essential (dhatiyyat) and accidental (aradiyyat) respectively. The author notes that 'essential' sometimes means only the internal. Then he clarifies the three terms: 'arad (accident), 'aradi (accidental universal), and mahal (subject). The majority distinguish them: the nature of 'arad when taken absolutely is 'aradi; when conditioned by a subject, it is mahal; when conditioned by nothing (i.e., as a separate concept), it is the accident opposite to substance. The author supports this with Ibn Sina's statement that accidents exist only in their subject. This guards against the misunderstanding that the accidental universal is the same as the accident.

الذاتي
العرضي
العرض
المحل

Key Takeaway
The universal divides into three types: identical to the individuals, essential (internal), and accidental (external); and 'arad (accident) is distinct from 'aradi (accidental universal).

Genus: The First Universal
Author introduces the five universals and defines genus with its condition of being an answer to 'what is it?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus in a given definition and determine whether it is proximate or distant.

Core Idea
Genus answers 'what is it?' and connects the species to its essence.

The passage introduces the five universals, focusing on the first: genus. Genus is not just any broad category; it must be predicated of many things that differ in reality, and it must answer the question 'what is it?' This condition makes genus a logical tool for definitions, not just a semantic grouping. Understanding genus is foundational to building correct definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Look for the term being defined. Here it is 'genus' (al-jins). The passage gives a definition: 'a universal predicated of many differing in realities, in answer to what is it?'
The definiendum is the first of the five universals.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The definition includes two parts: the broader category (universal) and the differentia (predicated of many differing in realities, in answer to what is it). Note the condition 'in answer to what is it' distinguishes genus from other universals like differentia or property.
The differentia here is not a simple property but a relational condition.
3
Distinguish proximate and distant
The passage adds a qualification: if the genus answers the complete essence and includes all participants, it is proximate (qarib); otherwise it is distant (ba'id). For example, 'animal' is proximate for man, but 'body' is distant because it does not capture the specific essence.
Proximate genus is the closest genus that shares the complete essence.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
In usul al-fiqh, definitions of legal terms often use genus. For instance, 'salah' is defined with genus 'ibadah' (worship) and differentia 'specific actions and words'. Here 'ibadah' is a proximate genus for salah.
This shows how genus functions in real Islamic science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالْكُلِّيَاتُ خَمْسٌ ، الْأَوَّلُ: الْجِنْسُ ، وَهُوَ: كُلّي، مَقُولٌ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ بِالْحَقَائِقِ، فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ، فَإِنْ كَانَ جَوَابًا عَنْ الْمَاهِيَّةِ وَجَمِيعِ الْمُشَارِكَاتِ فَقَرِيبٌ ، وَإِلَّا فبعيد.
The universals are five. The first: genus, and it is: a universal predicated of many differing in realities, in answer to 'what is it?' If it is an answer about the essence and all the participants, then it is proximate, otherwise distant.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage states that there are five universals (al-kulliyyat khams). The first is genus (jins). The definition given: genus is a universal predicated of many things that differ in their realities (i.e., different species) in answer to the question 'what is it?' (ma huwa). It then qualifies: if the genus answers the complete essence (al-mahiyya) and includes all participants of that essence, it is a proximate genus (qarib); otherwise it is a distant genus (ba'id). For example, 'animal' is a genus for man and horse; it answers 'what is it?' for both. If we ask 'what is man?' the answer 'animal' is proximate because it captures the genus directly; 'body' would be distant. This is standard definition in the tradition.

D) It is the opposite of differentia

Key Takeaway
Genus is a universal that answers 'what is it?' for many differing things, and its proximity depends on whether it captures the complete essence.

Analyzing the Question 'Ma Huwa'
Author analyzes the question 'ma huwa' and how it is answered in various cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of 'ma huwa' questions and identify the correct answer (species, definition, or genus) in a given context.

Core Idea
The question 'ma huwa' asks for the complete essence — specific or common.

In this passage, the author explains how to answer the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?). Depending on whether the question targets the specific essence or the common essence, and whether the things involved share one reality or differ, the answer can be the species, the complete definition, or the genus. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of essence
The passage distinguishes between al-mahiyya al-mukhtassa (specific essence) and al-mahiyya al-mushtaraka (common essence). Find these terms in the Arabic and note their meanings.
2
Determine the condition for the specific essence
If the question is about the specific essence and is restricted to one thing, then the answer is the species (al-naw') or the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm). 'One thing' implies a single species.
3
Determine the condition for the common essence
If the question is about the common essence that gathers multiple things, the answer differs: if those things share one reality (same species), answer with the species; if they differ in reality (different species), answer with the genus.
4
Extract the inference about two genera
The author deduces that it is impossible for one essence to have two genera at the same rank, because then 'ma huwa' would have two different answers, which is contradictory. Confirm this reasoning from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ مَا هُوَ سُؤَالٌ عَنْ تَمَامِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ إِنِ اقْتُصِرَ فِيهِ عَلَى أَمْرٍ وَاحِدٍ ، فَيُجَابُ بِالنَّوْعِ ، أَوْ بِالْحَدِّ التَّامَ، وَعَنْ تَمَامِ الْمَاهِيَّةِ الْمُشْتَرَكَةِ إِنْ جُمِعَ بَيْنَ أُمُورٍ ، فَيُجَابُ بِالنَّوْعِ، إِنْ كَانَتْ مُتَّفِقَةَ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَبِالْجِنْسِ إِنْ كَانَتْ مُخْتَلِفَتَهَا. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَقْتَرِحُ عَدَمُ إِمْكَانِ جِنْسَيْنِ فِي مَرْتَبَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ لِمَاهِيَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ.
And here are investigations. The first: that 'what is it?' is a question about the complete specific essence if it is restricted to one thing, then it is answered by the species, or by the complete definition; and about the complete common essence if it gathers between things, then it is answered by the species, if they are of one reality, and by the genus if they are of differing realities. And from this is inferred the impossibility of two genera at one rank for one essence.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the first investigation (mabahith) following the definition of genus. The author examines the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?). He says it asks about the complete essence. There are two cases: (1) If the question is about the specific essence restricted to one thing (i.e., a single species), then the answer is the species itself (al-naw') or the complete definition (al-hadd al-tamm). For example, if asked 'what is man?' the answer is 'rational animal' (complete definition) or 'man' (species). (2) If the question is about the common essence (al-mahiyya al-mushtaraka) that covers multiple things, then if those things share one reality, the answer is the species (like 'animal' for 'man' and 'horse'? Actually if they share one reality, they are the same species, so the answer is the species). If they differ in reality, the answer is the genus. For example, asking about man and horse gives 'animal' as genus. From this, the author deduces that there cannot be two genera at the same rank for a single essence, because one essence would have two answers to 'what is it?' which is impossible. This is a key point in logic: each species has exactly one proximate genus.

Key Terms
الماهية المختصة
الماهية المشتركة
النوع
الحد التام

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Genus
C) Differentia
D) Accident

Key Takeaway
The answer to 'ma huwa' must align with the type of essence and the reality of the things involved.

The Ontological Dependence of Genus on Species
Author argues genus exists only through species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why genus exists only through species, using the color example.

Core Idea
Genus has no independent existence; it is realized only through the actuality of its species.

In this investigation, the author clarifies a subtle point in the theory of universals: the ontological status of genus. While genus is logically prior to species, it does not have a separate existence. Understanding this helps prevent confusion in definition and classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find where the author states 'the existence of the genus is the existence of the species'. This is the claim that genus depends on species for its existence.
2
Understand the type of priority
The author notes that genus has priority over species, but not temporal priority. This is logical or conceptual priority: genus is a broader concept, but it does not exist before species in time.
3
Analyze the color example
When we think of color, we are not satisfied with an abstract concept; we need a specific color like white or black. The genus 'color' only becomes determinate through a species, showing its dependence.
4
Contrast with species
Unlike genus, the nature of the species does not need an additional determination to realize its meaning—it is already determinate. It only requires indication of an individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي: وُجُودُ الْجِنْسِ هُوَ وُجُودُ النَّوْعِ ذِهْنا وَخَارِجًا، فَهُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَيْهِ فِيهِمَا، وَمَنْشَأُ ذَلِكَ أَنَّ الْجِنْسَ لَيْسَ لَهُ تَحْصِيلٌ قَبْلَ النَّوْعِ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ قَبْلِيَّةٌ لَا بِالزَّمَانِ ; فَإِنَّ اللَّوْنَ - مَثَلًا - إِذَا أَخْطَرْنَاهُ بِالْبَالِ فَلَا يَقْنَعُ بِتَحَصُّلٍ شَيْءٍ مُتَقَرِّرٍ بِالْفِعْلِ، بَلْ يَطْلُبُ فِي مَعْنَى اللَّوْنِ زِيَادَةٌ ; حَتَّى يَتَقَرَّرَ بِتَقَرُّرِهِ بِالْفِعْلِ. وَأَمَّا طَبِيعَهُ النَّوْعِ فَلَيْسَ يَطْلُبُ فِيهَا تَحْصِيلَ مَعْنَاهَا ، بَلْ إِنَّمَا يَطْلُبُ تَحْصِيلَ الْإِشَارَةِ.
The second: The existence of the genus is the existence of the species, mentally and externally, so it is predicated upon it in both. And the source of that is that the genus has no realization before the species, even if there is priority not temporal. For example, color—when we bring it to mind—it is not satisfied with the realization of something actually determined, but rather it demands in the meaning of color an addition until it is determined by its actual determination. And as for the nature of the species, it does not demand in it the realization of its meaning, but rather it only demands the realization of indication.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is the second investigation about genus. The author argues that the genus exists only through the existence of the species, both in the mind and in external reality. The genus is predicated of the species in both domains. The reason is that the genus has no independent realization prior to the species; any priority is logical, not temporal. For instance, when we think of 'color', we are not satisfied with an abstract concept; we need a specific color (like white or black) to actually have a determinate instance. The genus 'color' is always accompanied by a specific color. In contrast, the nature of the species (like 'man') does not require something additional to realize its meaning; it is already determinate. The species only needs 'indication' (i.e., a reference to an individual). This shows that genus is ontologically dependent on species.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud): Existence; here used to discuss the ontological status of genus relative to species. [Note: Stable.]
التحصيل (al-tahsil): Realization or actualization; genus is not realized before species. [Note: Stable.]
القبلية (al-qabliyya): Priority; the genus has priority over species in a logical sense (conceptual), not temporal. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only through the existence of the species
C) Independently of the species
D) Only in the mind, not externally

Key Takeaway
Genus is ontologically dependent on species; it has no actuality except through the actual determination of its species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Distinguishing Genus from Matter: The Three Conditions
Resolving the difference between genus and matter via three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genus from matter by applying the three conditions (bi-shart 'adam, bi-shart ziyada, la bi-shart) to a given term.

Core Idea
Genus is matter without condition; matter is genus stripped of all form.

This passage addresses a logical puzzle: how can 'body' be both a genus for man (predicated) and also matter for man (not predicated)? The author resolves this by showing that 'body' can be considered under three different conditions, each yielding a different logical role: matter, species, or genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the puzzle
The passage begins with the apparent contradiction: body is said to be a genus for man (because it is predicated of man), yet also matter for man (and matter cannot be predicated). Note this tension.
2
Learn the three conditions
The author introduces three ways to consider 'body': (1) with the condition of no additional determination (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada), (2) with the condition of additional determination (bi-shart al-ziyada), (3) without any condition (la bi-shart). Each yields a different logical status.
3
Map each condition to its logical role
Body taken bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada is matter (not predicated), body taken bi-shart al-ziyada is species (e.g., man = body + rational soul), body taken la bi-shart is genus (predicated of any composite of matter and form).
4
Understand the practical implication
The genus is vague and unknown in form, yet predicated of all species. The matter is the underlying substrate without form. This distinction applies to both composite and simple essences, though the former is subtle and the latter problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : مَا الْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْجِنْسِ وَالْمَادَّةِ ; فَإِنَّهُ يُقَالُ لِلْجِسْمِ - مَثَلًا - إِنَّهُ جِنْسٌ لِلْإِنْسَانِ ، فَهُوَ مَحْمُولٌ عَلَيْهِ ، وَأَنَّهُ مَادَّةٌ لَهُ ، فَهُوَ مُسْتَحِيلُ الْحَمْلِ عَلَيْهِ؟ فَتَقُولُ: الْجِسْمُ الْمَأْخُوذُ بِشَرْطِ عَدَمِ الزَّيَادَةِ مَادَّةٌ ، وَالْمَأْخُوذُ بِشَرْطِ الزِّيَادَةِ نَوْعٌ، وَالْمَأْخُوذُ لَا بِشَرْطِ شَيْءٍ ، بَلْ كَيْفَمَا كَانَ، وَلَوْ مَعَ أَلْفِ مَعْنى مُقَوِّمٍ دَاخِلٍ في جُمْلَةِ تَحَصُّل مَعْنَاهُ جِنْسٌ ، فَهُوَ مَجْهُولٌ بَعْدُ ، لَا يُدْرَى أَنَّهُ عَلَى أَي صُورَةٍ ، وَمَحْمُولٌ عَلَى كُلِّ مُجْتَمِعٍ مِنْ مَادَّةٍ وَصُورَةٍ، وَاحِدَةٌ كَانَتْ أَوْ أَلْفًا. وَهَذَا عَامٌ فِيمَا ذَاتُهُ مِنْ مُرَكَّبَةٍ، وَفِيمَا ذَاتُهُ بَسِيطَةٌ، لَكِنْ فِي الْمُرَكَّبِ تَحْصِيلُ مَعْنى الْجِنسِ الْمُبْهَمِ عَسِيرٌ دَقِيقٌ ، وَفِي الْبَسِيطِ تَنْقِيحُ الْمَادَّةِ مُتَعَسِّرُ وَمُشْكِلٌ جِدًّا , فَإِنَّ ; إبْهَامَ الْمُتَعَيِّنِ وَتَعْيِينَ الْمُبْهَم أَمْرٌ عَظِيمٌ . وَهَذَا هُوَ الْفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الْفَضل وَالصُّورَةِ، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَسْمَعُهُمْ يَقُولُونَ: إِنَّ الْجِنْسَ مَأْخُوذُ مِنَ الْمَادَّةِ ، وَالفَصْلَ مَأخُوذٌ مِنَ الصُّورَةِ.
The third: What is the difference between genus and matter? For it is said of body, for example, that it is a genus for man, so it is predicated of him, and that it is matter for him, so it is impossible to be predicated of him? So you say: Body taken with the condition of absence of additional determination is matter; taken with the condition of additional determination is species; and taken without any condition, but however it may be, even with a thousand constituent meanings entering into the sum of the realization of its meaning, is genus, and it is still unknown, not known upon what form, and it is predicated of every composite of matter and form, whether one or a thousand. And this is general for what its essence is composite and for what its essence is simple. However, in the composite, the realization of the meaning of the vague genus is difficult and subtle, and in the simple, the refinement of the matter is very difficult and problematic, for the obscuring of the determinate and the determination of the vague is a great matter. And this is the difference between the differentia and the form, and hence you hear them saying: The genus is taken from the matter, and the differentia is taken from the form.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is the third investigation, addressing a common question: how can body be a genus for man (predicated of him) and also be matter for man (not predicated)? The resolution uses three conditions: (1) Body taken with the condition of no additional determination (bi-shart 'adam al-ziyada) is matter—it is not predicated because it lacks form. (2) Body taken with the condition of additional determination (bi-shart al-ziyada) is species—man is body + rational soul. (3) Body taken without any condition (la bi-shart shay') is genus—it is predicated of every composite of matter and form, whether one or many. The genus is 'vague' and unknown in form, while the differentia corresponds to form. This applies to both composite and simple essences, though the former is subtle and the latter problematic. The key insight: genus relates to matter, differentia to form.

Key Terms
المادة
الصورة
الشرط
بشرط عدم الزيادة
بشرط الزيادة
لا بشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When taken without any condition
B) When taken with the condition of no additional determination
C) When taken with the condition of additional determination
D) When taken as a genus

Key Takeaway
Genus, species, and matter are not different things but the same thing considered under different conditions (bi-shart 'adam, bi-shart ziyada, la bi-shart).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Universal as Genus of the Five
Author observes that the universal is the genus of the five universals, being more general and more specific than genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the universal is both more general and more specific than the genus, using the terms a'amm and akhass.

Core Idea
The universal is both more general and more specific than genus.

In this short but dense passage, the author reports a logical claim: the universal (al-kulli) is the genus of the five universals. He then introduces a paradox: the universal is both more general (a'amm) and more specific (akhass) than the genus. This seems contradictory, so we need to unravel the intended meaning by analyzing the relative scope of these terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the statement that the universal is the genus of the five universals. What does 'the five' refer to? (The five universals: genus, species, differentia, property, accident.)
2
Parse the comparative terms a'amm and akhass
The author says the universal is more general (a'amm) and more specific (akhass) than the genus. How can something be both? Think about two different respects: the universal is more general because it covers all five universals, but it is more specific because it is itself one of the five (as a genus?). The text is incomplete, but the idea is that the universal is a genus for the five, yet the genus is a species under the universal? This is the logical nuance to explore.
3
Consider the relative scope
Reflect: The universal as a concept includes all five universals as species. So it is more general than any single one. But the genus (jins) is itself one of the five, and the universal is a genus that includes the genus. So the universal is more specific than the genus because it is a higher-order genus? Actually, the phrase 'more specific than the genus' suggests that the universal is a narrower concept in some sense. Perhaps the intended meaning is that the universal is more general than the genus in extension (it includes more things) but more specific than the genus in intension (it has additional meaning)? This is a subtle point; don't worry if it's not fully clear from this passage alone.
4
Apply the concept to an Islamic science example
Consider the term 'hukm' (ruling) as a universal. It includes wajib, haram, etc. Is 'hukm' more general or more specific than 'wajib'? Clearly more general. But can 'hukm' be more specific than 'wajib' in some respect? That would be unusual. The paradoxical nature of the universal's relationship to the genus is a logical curiosity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ: قَالُوا إِنَّ الْكُلّيَّ جِنْسُ الْخَمْسَةِ، فَهُوَ أَعَمُ وَأَخَصُّ مِنَ الْجِنْسِ
The fourth: They said that the universal is the genus of the five, so it is more general and more specific than the genus.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This brief passage begins the fourth investigation. The author reports a claim: the universal (al-kulli) is the genus of the five universals (i.e., it is a broader category that includes them). However, he notes that the universal is both more general (a'amm) and more specific (akhass) than the genus (jins). This is because the universal is a genus for the five universals, but the term 'universal' is also applied to other things? The text cuts off, but the idea is that the universal is a higher genus for the five, yet in another sense it is more specific than the genus (presumably because it is a genus only for these five, while the genus is a more specific term within the five). This is a typical logical nuance about relative generality.
