Three Considerations of the Natural Universal: Abstract, Mixed, Absolute
Author subdivides natural universal into three considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the abstract, mixed, and absolute considerations of the natural universal and explain why the absolute is neither existent nor non-existent.

Core Idea
The natural universal can be considered with nothing, with something, or without any condition — each yielding a distinct logical status.

The natural universal, the essence as it exists in individuals, can be examined under three conditions: stripped of all attributes (abstract), combined with some attribute (mixed), or taken without any condition (absolute). The absolute is unique in that it transcends existence and non-existence. This breakdown sharpens our understanding of definitions and the metaphysical status of universals.

Guided Path
1
Recall the natural universal
The natural universal is the essence that exists in individuals, such as 'humanity' in every human.
It is the subject of the concept (معروض المفهوم).
2
Consider the natural with the condition of nothing (abstract)
This is the natural universal considered with the condition that no attribute is attached. For 'humanity', this would be humanity stripped of all specific properties.
It is called mujarrada (abstracted).
3
Consider the natural with the condition of something (mixed)
This is the natural universal considered together with some accident or attribute. For 'humanity', this could be humanity as the subject of rationality or as existing in time.
It is called makhluta (mixed).
4
Consider the natural without any condition (absolute)
This is the natural universal taken without any condition whatsoever. For 'humanity', it is neither existent nor non-existent; it is the essence in itself, beyond the law of excluded middle.
It is called mutlaqa (absolute). The passage says 'in this rank the two contradictories are removed.'
5
Compare natural and absolute
The natural universal is more general than the absolute, because the absolute is a specific way of considering the natural. The absolute does not entail dividing the thing into itself and other.
This avoids logical circularity.

Source Text

The concept of the universal is called 'logical universal', and the subject of that concept is called 'natural universal', and the combination of the attribute and the subject is called 'rational universal'. Then the natural universal has three considerations: with the condition of nothing, called 'abstract' (mujarrada); with the condition of something, called 'mixed' (makhluta); and without condition of anything, called 'absolute' (mutlaqa). And it (the absolute) in itself is neither existent nor non-existent nor any of the accidents, so in this rank the two contradictories are removed. And the natural is more general in one respect than the absolute, so it does not entail dividing a thing into itself and other.

The Abstract Universal: Existence and Plato's Error
Author denies external existence of abstract universal except for Plato, affirms mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the abstract universal (mujarrada) from the mixed (makhluta) and absolute (mutlaqa), and evaluate Plato's claim about its external existence using the Islamic logical framework.

Core Idea
The abstract universal exists only in the mind—Plato's error was to place it outside.

In the study of the five universals, logicians distinguish three modes: mixed (with matter), absolute (no condition), and abstract (stripped of all attributes). This passage clarifies that the abstract universal has no external reality; only Plato claimed otherwise. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how Islamic logic treats universals as mental concepts rather than separate entities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes mentioned
The passage begins by referencing 'the mixed and the absolute' before discussing the abstract. These are the three ways the natural universal is considered: mixed (with particularized matter), absolute (with no condition), and abstract (with the condition of nothing).
The mixed and absolute are not the focus here; they provide context for the abstract.
2
Define the abstract universal (mujarrada)
The abstract universal is the natural universal considered 'with the condition of nothing' — stripped of all attributes, including universality itself. It is a pure concept in the mind.
It is also called the 'separated' universal, but not in the sense of external separation.
3
Locate Plato's claim
The passage states that only Plato held that the abstract universal exists externally. These are the Platonic Forms (al-muthul al-aflatuniyya), which he believed were self-subsistent realities separate from particulars.
This is presented as a point of reproach against Plato in the Islamic logical tradition.
4
Analyze the author's refutation
The author says 'no one has held its external existence except Plato' and that this is 'one of the things for which he is reproached.' The refutation is by consensus: the external existence of the abstract universal is rejected because only Plato held it, and it contradicts the nature of universals as mental constructs.
The author does not give a detailed argument here; he relies on the accepted view.
5
Affirm mental existence
The passage asks whether the abstract universal exists in the mind. Some said no, but the author affirms yes, 'for there is no restriction in conceptualizations.' The mind can conceive of abstract universals without needing external counterparts.
This is the correct view in Islamic logic: universals have mental reality (wujud dhihni).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا فِي الْمَخْلُوطَةِ وَالْمُطْلَقَةِ ، وَأَمَّا الْمُجَرَّدَةُ فَلَمْ يَذْهَبْ أَحَدٌ إِلَى وُجُودِهَا الْخَارِج ، إِلَّا أَفَلَاطُونُ ، وَهِيَ الْمُثْلُ الْأَفَلَا طُونِيَّةُ ، وَهَذَا مِمَّا يُشَنَّعُ بِهِ عَلَيْهِ. وَهَلْ تُوجَدُ الْمُجَرَّدَةُ فِي الذَّهْنِ ؟ قِيلَ لَا ، وَقِيلَ نَعَمْ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ؛ فَإِنَّهُ لَا حَجْرَ فِي التَّصَرُّرَاتِ .
This is concerning the mixed (makhluta) and the absolute (mutlaqa). As for the abstract (mujarrada), no one has held its external existence except Plato, and they are the Platonic Forms, and this is one of the things for which he is reproached. And does the abstract exist in the mind? Some said no, some said yes, and this (yes) is the truth, for there is no restriction in conceptualizations.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a critical note on the abstract universal (mujarrada), which is the natural universal considered with the condition of nothing, i.e., stripped of all attributes. The author states that no one claimed its external existence except Plato, who posited the Platonic Forms—separate, abstract realities. This is a point of criticism against Plato. The author then addresses the question of mental existence: some denied mental existence for the abstract universal, but the correct view is that it does exist in the mind, because conceptualization is not restricted to external objects; the mind can conceive of abstract universals.

Key Terms
الْمُجَرَّدَة
الْمُثْل الْأَفَلَا طُونِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The abstract universal exists only in the mind; Plato's error of placing it externally is rejected in Islamic logic by affirming mental existence and denying any external counterpart.

The Four Relations Between Singular Terms (al-Nisab al-Arbaʿ)
Introduces the four relations between individuals based on truthfulness about something.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any pair of singular terms into one of the four relations (equality, inclusion, exclusion, overlap) based on truthfulness about a subject.

Core Idea
Four relations determine how singular terms connect: equality, inclusion, exclusion, and overlap.

In this lesson, we explore the four relations (al-nisab al-arbaʿ) that hold between singular terms (mufradāt) when they are predicated of something. The basis is 'truthfulness about something' (al-sidq ʿalā shayʾ) — comparing whether both terms can be truly said of the same subject. This tool is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis
The relation between two singular terms is determined by whether both can be truly predicated of the same subject. This is called 'al-sidq ʿalā shayʾ'.
2
Learn the four categories
The four relations are: equality (musāwāt: both terms are true of the same subjects and entirely coextensive), inclusion (kullī wa juzʾī: one term is true of all subjects of the other but not vice versa), exclusion (mubāyanah: no subject can be described by both terms), and overlap (umūm min wajh: some subjects are described by both, some by only one).
These correspond to the classical relations of coextension, subordination, contradiction, and intersection.
3
Apply to an example from Islamic sciences
Take the terms 'insān' (human) and 'nāṭiq' (rational). In classical logic, they are often considered equal (every human is rational, every rational is human). Now take 'insān' and 'ḥayawān' (animal): every human is an animal, but not every animal is human – this is inclusion. Take 'insān' and 'faras' (horse): no human is a horse – exclusion. Take 'insān' and 'tājir' (merchant): some humans are merchants, some are not – overlap.
Ensure the examples are from Islamic discourse, not Greek.
4
Check your classification
For a given pair, ask: (1) Can both be true of the same thing? (2) If yes, are all things that are A also B? (3) Are all things that are B also A? (4) If neither is fully included in the other, they overlap.
Use truthfulness, not meaning similarity.

Three Types of Difference and the Tashkik Debate in Essence
Author classifies three types of difference and presents the Ishraqi vs Mashsha'i disagreement on gradation in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of difference (ikhtilaf) and explain the Ishraqi vs. Mashsha'i disagreement on tashkik in mahiyyah.

Core Idea
Difference by perfection and deficiency in the same essence is the key point of contention between Illuminationists and Peripatetics.

In classical metaphysics, understanding how things differ is foundational. The author first distinguishes three modes of difference: essential, accidental, and gradational. The third mode is controversial: can one essence exceed itself in perfection? The Illuminationists affirm it; the Peripatetics deny it. This lesson parses the classification and the debate, equipping you to recognize and analyze arguments about gradation in essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of difference
Locate the three types listed: (1) difference in essence (e.g., man vs. horse), (2) difference in accidents (e.g., black vs. Roman), (3) difference of essence by perfection and deficiency. Memorize their definitions and examples.
The Arabic uses 'ikhtilaf bi al-mahiyyah', 'ikhtilaf bi al-'awarid', 'ikhtilaf al-mahiyyah bi al-kamal wa al-nuqṣan'.
2
Understand the disputed third type
Read the definition: the essence exceeding itself in one mode of existence over another without a mediating factor. Note that this applies to cause-effect and quantitative gradation (e.g., line length, blackness intensity).
The key phrase: 'كونها في نحو من الوجود زائدا بنفسها على نفسها في نحو آخر من الوجود، من دون واسطة في العروض'.
3
Locate the schools' positions
Find where the author states: the Illuminationists (Ishraqiyyun) affirmed tashkik in mahiyyah, the Peripatetics (Mashsha'un) denied it. They agreed on the first two types.
The author cites 'Allamah al-Shirazi for the point of dispute.
4
Analyze the Illuminationist arguments
Examine the quote from al-Sadr al-Shirazi: they allowed tashkik in all modes. For the first mode (priority of cause), they argued existence of cause is more deserving without being a requirement of essence. For the last two, they gave examples like magnitude increasing by itself.
This shows the Illuminationists interpret tashkik broadly, including priority and intensity.
5
Assess the Peripatetic position
The Peripatetics deny tashkik in mahiyyah because for them essence is univocal; any gradation must be due to accidents or external factors. The passage does not elaborate their arguments but implies they reject the third type.
The disagreement is metaphysical, not merely logical.

فصل في الألفاظ لطالب الصواب الأول: هل في الماهية تشكيك أم لا ؟ فالإشراقية مالوا إلى الأول ، والمشاؤون إلى الثاني، وتحرير محل النزاع على ما يفهم من كلام العلامة الشيرازي أن الاختلاف بين الأشياء يتصور على أنحاء: اختلاف بالماهية كما بين الإنسان والفرس، واختلاف بالعوارض، كما بين الزنجي والرومي، واختلاف الماهية بالكمال والنقصان . فبعد الاتفاق على الأولين اختلفوا في الثالث، فالإشراقية أثبتوه ، والمشاؤون نفوه والاختلاف بالكمال والنقصان كونها في نحو من الوجود زائدا بنفسها على نفسها في نحو آخر من الوجود، من دون واسطة في العروض، هذا في النحوين الأخيرين، أما النحو الأول فلا يتصور في الذاتي، وهو الأشبه؛ لأن الذاتي لا يكون مقتضى الذات، وأما الثاني فالأشبه جوازه في الذاتي والماهية ؛ لأن العلة والمعلول قد يكونان من نوع واحد، فلا بد من أن يكون العلة بماهيتها مقدمة على ماهية المعلول ؛ على ما ينادى عليه الجعل البسيط . وإذ ماهيتهما واحدة فلا بد أن تكون هي في نحو من الوجود مقدمة على نفسها في نحو آخر منه، ومصداق حمل النوع عليهما ذاتاهما مع كون أحدهما متقدما على الآخر، فصدق النوع على بعض أفراده، وهو على صدقه على بعض آخر ، وهو المعلول ، قال الصدر الشيرازي: الإشراقية جوزوا التشكيك في الماهية بجميع أنحائها، فالجواز بالنحو الثاني قد عرفت. وأما النحو الأول فلعله لأنهم لم يفسروه بما ذكر؛ لأنهم قالوا: وجود العلة أولى من وجود المعلول، مع عدم كونه مقتضى الذات، وهذا النحو من الأولوية يجوز في الماهية بلا ريب، وأما جواز النحوين الأخيرين فلأن المقدار إنما يزيد على مقدار آخر بنفسه، كالخط بنفس الخط يزيد على آخر، وكذا السواد الشديد على الضعيف .
Chapter on words. The first issue for the seeker of truth: Is there tashkik (gradation) in mahiyyah (essence) or not? The Illuminationists inclined to the first, and the Peripatetics to the second. The clarification of the point of dispute, as understood from the words of 'Allamah al-Shirazi, is that difference between things is conceivable in modes: difference in essence, as between man and horse; difference in accidents, as between a black person and a Roman; and difference of essence by perfection and deficiency. After agreement on the first two, they disagreed on the third. The Illuminationists affirmed it, and the Peripatetics denied it. Difference by perfection and deficiency is the thing's being, in one mode of existence, exceeding itself by itself in another mode of existence, without a mediating factor in attribution. This [applies] to the last two modes. As for the first mode, it is not conceivable in the essential (al-dhati), and that is more appropriate because the essential is not a requirement of the essence. As for the second mode, its permissibility in the essential and essence is more appropriate because cause and effect may be of one type; thus the cause must in its essence be prior to the essence of the effect, as indicated by simple making (al-ja'l al-basit). And since their essences are one, it must be that it [the essence] is in one mode of existence prior to itself in another mode. The instance of the predication of the type on both of them is their own essences, with one of them being prior to the other. So the truth of the type on some of its individuals is prior to its truth on others, and that [latter] is the effect. al-Sadr al-Shirazi said: The Illuminationists allowed tashkik in the essence in all its modes. The permissibility of the second mode you have known. As for the first mode, perhaps because they did not interpret it as mentioned; because they said: the existence of the cause is more deserving than the existence of the effect, without it being a requirement of the essence, and this mode of priority is permissible in essence without doubt. As for the permissibility of the last two modes, because a magnitude increases over another magnitude by itself, like a line by itself increases over another line, and similarly intense blackness over weak blackness.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage. The author opens a chapter on words (alfaz) with the question of whether there is tashkik (gradation) in mahiyyah (essence). He states the two opposing schools: the Illuminationists (Ishraqiyyun) affirm it, the Peripatetics (Mashsha'un) deny it. He then clarifies the point of dispute by distinguishing three types of difference: (1) difference in essence (e.g., man vs. horse), (2) difference in accidents (e.g., black vs. Roman), and (3) difference of essence by perfection and deficiency (i.e., gradation). Both schools agree on the first two, but disagree on the third. The definition of the third type is given: a thing in one mode of existence exceeds itself in another mode without any mediating factor in attribution. The author then discusses the applicability of these types to essential (dhati) and accidental. The first type is not conceivable in essential because the essential does not necessitate essence. The second type is permissible in essential and essence, using the example of cause and effect being of the same type. He quotes al-Sadr al-Shirazi who explains the Illuminationist position: they allowed tashkik in essence in all its modes. The first mode (the Illuminationist interpretation) is based on the priority of cause without being a requirement of essence, and the last two modes are supported by examples like magnitude increasing by itself and blackness intensifying.

Where Does Tashkik Occur? Essences vs. Accidents
Explains that tashkik occurs in accidental attributes, not in essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tashkik in accidental attributes and tashkik in essences, and apply the Peripatetic argument to determine the locus of gradation in any given Islamic philosophical or theological concept.

Core Idea
Tashkik applies to accidental attributes, not to essences, whether substantial or accidental.

In this passage, the commentator presents the Peripatetic stance on tashkik (gradational predication). Tashkik refers to terms that apply to their instances with varying degrees, like 'white' applying more or less to different objects. The key question is whether this gradation is inherent in the essence itself or only in the accidental attributes. The Peripatetics argue that essences (whether substantial like 'body' or accidental like 'blackness') do not admit of tashkik; rather, gradation occurs only in the attribution of accidents to individual subjects. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions and predicates in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Define tashkik and its key features
Tashkik is a type of predication where the same term applies to multiple instances with differing intensity—for example, 'whiteness' applies more to snow than to milk. Confirm the definition from the passage and note how it differs from univocal (mutawati') and equivocal (mushtarak) terms.
Tashkik is sometimes called analogical predication.
2
Distinguish between substantial and accidental essences
The passage states that tashkik does not occur in substantial essences (like 'body') nor in accidental essences (like 'blackness' as a universal). Understand that accidental essences are still essences, not individual accidents. For example, 'blackness' as a universal is an accidental essence, but a particular black object is an instance.
Mahiyya (essence) here refers to the quiddity, not the individual.
3
Analyze al-Dawani's proof using conditional reasoning
Al-Dawani argues: If an essence could be more or less, then either the more includes something the less lacks (first branch) or not (second branch). If not, there is no difference. If it includes something, that extra is either a differentia (fasl) making them different species, or an accident, meaning tashkik is in the accident, not in the essence. Work through each branch to see why tashkik cannot be in the essence itself.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against tashkik in mahiyya.
4
Apply to examples from Islamic sciences
Consider 'iman' (faith): does it admit degrees? According to the Peripatetics, the essence of iman (as a genus) does not have degrees, but the strength of conviction (an accidental attribute) does. Similarly, 'ilm' (knowledge) – the essence of knowledge is not graded, but the certainty or clarity of knowledge is. Test the distinction on other concepts like 'wujood' (existence) or 'qudra' (power).
This analysis is used in kalām to debate whether faith increases or decreases.

بالفصول المُنوّعة ، وهو فاسد كما سيظهر إن شاء الله ، واختار المصنف مذهب المشائين ، وقال: (وَلَا تَشْكِيكَ فِي الْمَاهِيَّاتِ الجوهرية، (وَلَا فِي الْعَوَارِضِ)، أي الماهيات العرضية ، (بَلْ فِي اتِّصَافِ الْأَفْرَادِ)، أي أفراد الماهية (بها)، أي بالعوارض، (فَلَا تَشْكِيكَ فِي الْجِسْمِ) ؛ لأنه ماهية جوهرية، (وَلَا في السَّوَادِ)؛ لأنه ماهية عرضية.
(بَلْ) التشكيك ([في اتِّصَافِ أَفْرَادِهِ بِهِ في أَسْوَدَ)، وأوثق دلائلهم ما قال المحقق الدواني رحمه الله تعالى : لو كان الماهية زائدة أو ناقصة أو شديدة أو ضعيفة ، فإما أن يشتمل الشديد الزائد على أمر ليس في الضعيف والناقص، لم يشتمل، وعلى الثاني لم يكن فرق بينهما وبين مقابليهما، وعلى الأول فذلك الأمر إما فصل، فاختلف الشديد والضعيف ،نوعا، فلا يكون الماهية شديدة أو ضعيفة، وكذا حال الزائد والناقص ، أو عارض فيكون التشكيك في العارض ، لا فيها .
ولا يرد النقض بالعارض، بأن الشديد منه إن اشتمل على أمر ليس في الضعيف، فإما فصل ،له، فاختلفا نوعا أو عارض فلا يكون في العارض المفروض تشكيك ، بل في عارض وإن لم يشتمل، فلا فرق؛ لأنا نختار الشق الأخير، ونقول: يجوز أن يكون فيه فرق باعتبار المصداق؛ إذ المصداق في صدق المشتق قيام المبدأ، وباختلافه شدة وضعفا، ولو كان هذا الاختلاف فيه بالنوع يختلف المصداق، فيختلف الصدق بخلاف الذاتي ؛ فإن المصداق فيه نفس الماهية. وفيه نظر، أما :أولا فلأن اختلاف الشديد والضعيف بالماهية لا ينفي التشكيك في الماهية الجنسية؛ لجواز أن يكون الفصول واسطة في الثبوت .
وأما ثانيا : فلأن اشتمال الشديد على عارض ليس في الضعيف لا يوجب التشكيك في العارض فقط، بل يجوز أن يكون الماهية بنفسها شديدة أو ضعيفة، ويكون العارض واسطة في الثبوت فقط ، فتأمل فيهما.
وأما ثالثا فلأن عدم اشتمال الشديد والزائد على أمر ليس في الناقص والضعيف لا يوجب عدم الفرق بينهما ؛ لجواز أن يكون الفرق بنحو من الوجود، فيكون الماهية بنفسها في نحو من الوجود زائدة أو شديدة، وفي نحو آخر ناقصة أو ضعيفة.
ثم إنه قال الجامع بين المعقول والمنقول، بحر العلوم، صاحب المقامات الرفيعة أبونا وأستاذنا نظام الملة والدين، أغرقه الله في بحار رحمته وأسكنه في مقام خلته في تعليقاته على الحواشي القديمة»: يمكن توجيه كلام المشائيين بأن الشدة صدق الكلي على موضوع واحد بأصداق كثيرة، والضعف ضده، وذا لا يمكن في الذاتي ؛ فإن الصدق لا يتكثر إلا بتكثر المصداق، ولو تكثر المصداق في صدق الذاتي لتكثر ذات الموضوع ؛ لأنها المصداق، هذا خلف، بخلاف العارض؛ إذ المصداق فيه هو المبدأ القائم بالموضوع، فإذا كان شديدا أمكن انتزاع أمثال الضعيف، فيتكرر الحمل بحسبها، ثم قال: وعلى هذا يصير النزاع بين الفريقين لفظيا. وقال أيضا: ويأبى عنه كلمات المشائين أيضا، وقد ظهر لك من هذا القيل والقال أن الحق ما قال الإشراقية، ولا يصح قول المشائين إلا بالتأويل بنحو آخر ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Section on expressions. By the differentiating differentiae, and it is corrupt as will appear if God wills. And the author chose the doctrine of the Peripatetics and said: 'There is no tashkik in substantial essences, nor in accidents,' i.e., accidental essences, 'but in the attribution of individuals,' i.e., individuals of the essence, 'with them,' i.e., with accidents, 'so there is no tashkik in body' because it is a substantial essence, 'nor in blackness' because it is an accidental essence. 'But' tashkik is in the attribution of its individuals to it, like black. And the strongest of their proofs is what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani (may God have mercy on him) said: If the essence were more or less or intense or weak, then either the intense and more includes something that the weak and less does not, or it does not include it. On the second, there would be no difference between them and their opposites. On the first, that thing is either a differentia, then the intense and the weak would differ in species, so the essence would not be intense or weak, and likewise for the more and less; or it is an accident, so tashkik would be in the accident, not in it. And the objection against the accident is not refuted, that the intense of it, if it includes something not in the weak, then either it is a differentia for it, so they differ in species, or an accident, so there is no tashkik in the supposed accident but in an accident, and if it does not include, there is no difference. For we choose the last alternative and say: It is possible that there is a difference with respect to the referent, for the referent in the truth of the derivative is the subsistence of the principle, and by its difference of intensity and weakness, and if this difference were in kind, the referent would differ, so the truth would differ unlike the essential, for the referent in it is the essence itself. And there is a consideration: First, because the difference between the intense and weak in essence does not negate tashkik in the generic essence; for it is possible that the differentiae are intermediate in existence. Second, because the inclusion of the intense of an accident not in the weak does not necessitate tashkik in the accident only, but it is possible that the essence by itself is intense or weak, and the accident is only intermediate in existence. So reflect on them. Third, because the non-inclusion of the intense and more of something not in the less and weak does not necessitate the absence of difference between them; for it is possible that the difference is by a mode of existence, so the essence by itself in one mode of existence is more or intense, and in another mode less or weak. Then he who combines the rational and transmitted, Bahr al-Ulum, possessor of lofty stations, our father and teacher, the exemplar of the community and religion, may God immerse him in seas of His mercy and settle him in the station of His love, said in his glosses on the old supercommentaries: 'The words of the Peripatetics can be reconciled by saying: intensity is the predication of the universal of one subject with many individual attributes, and weakness is its opposite, and that is not possible in the essential, for predication does not multiply except by multiplication of the referent, and if the referent multiplied in the predication of the essential, the subject itself would multiply because it is the referent, which is absurd, unlike the accident, for the referent in it is the principle subsisting in the subject, so if it is intense, it is possible to extract instances of the weak, so the predication repeats accordingly.' Then he said: 'On this the dispute between the two groups becomes verbal.' And he also said: 'But the words of the Peripatetics reject it as well, and it has become clear to you from this discussion that the truth is what the Illuminationists said, and the Peripatetics' statement is not valid except through reinterpretation in another way, and God knows best what is correct.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that clarifies the doctrine of the Peripatetics regarding tashkik (gradation or analogy) and defends it while also presenting criticisms. The author begins by stating the Peripatetic position: tashkik does not occur in essential or accidental essences themselves, but only in the attribution of accidental properties to individual instances. For example, 'body' as a substantial essence does not admit of tashkik (there is no more or less of 'bodyness'), and similarly 'blackness' as an accidental essence does not admit of tashkik. Rather, tashkik applies to the attribution of blackness to individual objects (some black objects are more black than others). The author then presents the strongest proof for this view, attributed to al-Dawani: if an essence itself could be more or less, intense or weak, then either the intense includes something the weak lacks or it does not. If it does not, there is no difference, so tashkik is meaningless. If it does include something extra, that extra is either a differentia (fasl) — making the intense and weak different species — or an accident — in which case the tashkik is in the accident, not in the essence. Thus, tashkik is not in the essence. A potential objection is raised: what if the intense includes an extra accident that the weak lacks? The commentator responds by choosing a different alternative: the difference could be in the referent (masdaq) of the derivative term. For accidentals, the referent is the accident's instantiation in the subject, which can vary in intensity. But for essentials, the referent is the essence itself, so no such variation occurs. However, the commentator finds three points of criticism in this proof. First, the difference in intensity within a species does not negate tashkik in a generic essence, since different species could share a genus and still have different degrees. Second, even if the intense includes an extra accident, the essence itself could still be intrinsically intense or weak with the accident merely mediating. Third, the lack of a component difference does not rule out modal differences in existence; the essence could be intense in one mode and weak in another. The author then quotes his own earlier gloss (Bahr al-Ulum's commentary on older marginalia) offering a reconciliation: intensity and weakness are about the frequency of predication — a single subject can have multiple instances of the accident, allowing many predications, whereas essentials have only one predication. This might make the dispute verbal. However, he concludes that the Illuminationist position (that tashkik does apply to essences in some sense) is the correct one, and the Peripatetic view requires reinterpretation to be valid.

Key Takeaway
Tashkik (gradational predication) occurs in accidental attributes, not in essences, as per the Peripatetic doctrine defended in this passage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'nur' (light) as used in the Qur'an: 'Allah is the light of the heavens and the earth' and also 'light is found in a lamp'. Does this application exhibit tashkik? If so, is the tashkik in the essence of light or in the attribution? Use the Peripatetic argument to decide.
?
In fiqh, the term 'hurma' (prohibition) is applied to both greater and lesser sins. Does this involve tashkik? Justify your answer based on the passage's distinction.

The Criterion for Universal vs. Particular Concepts
Author defines the concept and divides it into universal and particular based on mental permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether any given concept is universal or particular by applying the mental multiplicity test.

Core Idea
A concept's universality depends on mental possibility of many individuals, not on actual existence.

This lesson introduces the most fundamental division in logic: the division of concepts into universal and particular. The author provides a simple yet precise criterion: if the mind can conceive of multiple individuals falling under a concept, it is universal; if not, it is particular. This test is purely mental and does not depend on whether those individuals exist in reality. You will learn how to apply this test and why hypothetical universals—concepts with no possible individuals—are still considered universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept in question.
Start by clearly defining the concept you want to test. For example, take the concept 'human' (إنسان).
The concept must be understood as a mental representation, not a specific individual.
2
Apply the mental permission test.
Ask: Can the mind conceive of multiple individuals that fit this concept? For 'human', yes (many humans). For 'the current Caliph' (assuming only one), no. So 'human' permits multiplicity; the specific title does not.
This is a mental test, not a count of actual instances.
3
Classify the concept.
If the answer is yes, the concept is universal (كلي). If no, it is particular (جزئي). 'Human' is universal; 'the current Caliph' is particular.
4
Consider hypothetical universals.
Some concepts permit multiplicity in the mind even though they have no actual individuals, e.g., 'the impossible' or 'partner of Allah'. They are still universal because the mind can conceive of many instances hypothetically.
This shows that the criterion is purely conceptual, not existential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَفْهُومُ إِنْ جَوَّزَ الْعَقْلُ تَكَثُرَهُ، أَيْ تَكْثُرَ ذَلِكَ الْمَفْهُومُ بِاعْتِبَارِ صِدْقِهِ إِلَى جُزْئِيٍّ وَكُلِّيٍّ عَلَى الْأَفْرَادِ مُوَاطَاةً تَجْوِيزًا، مِنْ حَيْثُ تَصَوُّرُهُ، أَيْ ذَلِكَ الْمَفْهُومُ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ أَمْرٍ خَارِجٍ، فَكُلِّيٌّ، مُمْتَنِعٌ أَفْرَادُهُ، كَالْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ.
The concept: if the mind allows its multiplicity, i.e., that concept multiplies in consideration of its truth to particulars and universals upon individuals by way of correspondence through permission, from the perspective of its conception, i.e., that concept while disregarding an external matter, then it is universal. [If the mind does not allow multiplicity, then it is particular.] And [the universal] whose individuals are impossible, like the hypothetical universals.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage explains the division of the concept (المفهوم) into universal (كلي) and particular (جزئي). The criterion is whether the mind can conceive of multiple individuals falling under that concept. If it can, the concept is universal; if not, it is particular. This is considered purely from the perspective of the concept itself, without reference to external existence. The author notes that some universals have no actual individuals (أفراد ممتنعة), like hypothetical universals (الكليات الفرضية). This leads to the classification of the universal into those with actual individuals and those without.

Critique of the Dispersed Individual (al-Fard al-Muntashir)
Author critiques the concept of dispersed individual and denies its external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical problems with the concept of the dispersed individual and evaluate why it has no external existence according to the author.

Core Idea
The dispersed individual is a mental construct, not an external reality.

This passage presents a critical examination of the concept of the dispersed individual, a supposed universal that can be shared alternately but not collectively. The author systematically refutes its external existence by showing that any attempt to restrict a universal either fails to produce particularity or leads to contradictions. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the Islamic logical position on the nature of universals and particulars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible restrictions
The author begins by considering how a universal can be restricted: either by another universal (no particularity), by a particular that cannot be shared (makes the restricted also non-shareable), or by a particular that can be shared alternately (the dispersed).
The first two are dismissed as not producing a dispersed individual.
2
Analyze the definition of the dispersed individual
The dispersed individual is defined as a universal displayed to a specific person whose sharing is prevented collectively but allowed alternately. Contrast this with a non-dispersed particular whose unique essence prevents any sharing.
Note the key terms: 'تعرض' (displayed) and 'على البدل' (alternately).
3
Examine the author's objection to its external existence
The author argues that if the dispersed individual existed in all persons, it would be universal (sharing collectively); if in only some, that would be arbitrary (preference without preponderant). Thus it cannot exist externally.
This is a logical dilemma.
4
Conclude with the author's verdict
The truth is that the dispersed individual has no existence in external entities (a'yan). It is only observed by the senses as a thing that, if it existed externally, would be one of the individuals alternately. If this mental consideration is what they intend, it is fine; otherwise it is baseless.
The author accepts a purely mental interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلي، والتقيد إما كلي فلا يفيد الجزئية، أو جزئي غير قابل للاشتراك، فيكون المقيد أيضا كذلك، أو قابل له على البدل، وهو منتشر، وينجر الكلام في انتشاره، ولا يخفى فساده؛ لأن الفرد المنتشر عبارة عن الكلي المعروض لشخص خاص يمتنع اشتراكه اجتماعا لا بدلا كما أن الجزئي الغير المنتشر عبارة عنه، من حيث هو معروض الهذية المانعة عنه بالكلية، والتشخص أمر عدمي، والكلي غير مقيد به، ولو سلم أنه كلي مقيد فنقول: إنه مقيد بتشخص هو منتشر بنفسه، كما أن التشخص المانع من الشركة بالكلية متعين بنفسه عند من يراه أمرا موجودا. نعم، يرد على الفرد المنتشر أن وجوده في جميع الأشخاص يوجب الشركة على الاجتماع وعليها مدار الكلية ووجوده في البعض دون بعض ترجيح من غير مرجح، فإذن الحق أن لا وجود له في الأعيان، وإنما يلاحظ بواسطة الحاسة أمر لو وجد في الأعيان لكان عين واحد من الأفراد على البدل، فإن كان مقصودهم هذا فلا غبار عليه، وإلا فغير محصل.
The universal, and the restriction: either it is universal, so it does not benefit particularity, or it is particular not capable of sharing, so the restricted is also like that, or it is capable of it alternately, and it is the dispersed one, and discussion continues about its being dispersed, and its corruption is not hidden; because the dispersed individual is the universal displayed to a specific person whose sharing is prevented collectively, not alternately, just as the non-dispersed particular is that which is, in terms of being displayed to the essence that prevents [sharing] completely, and individuation is a non-entity, and the universal is not restricted by it, and if it is granted that it is a restricted universal, we say: it is restricted by an individuation that is itself dispersed, just as the individuation that prevents sharing completely is itself determined according to one who sees it as an existent thing. Yes, what is directed at the dispersed individual is that its existence in all persons necessitates sharing collectively, and on it depends universality, and its existence in some without others is a preference without a preponderant, so then the truth is that it has no existence in external entities, and it is only observed by means of the sense as a thing that, if it existed in external entities, would be exactly one of the individuals alternately; if what they intend is this, then there is no fault in it, otherwise it is not attained.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the concept of the dispersed individual (al-fard al-muntashir). The author begins by considering how a universal can be restricted: either by another universal (which does not produce particularity), or by a particular that cannot be shared (making the restricted also non-shareable), or by a particular that can be shared alternately (the dispersed). He immediately declares this last option corrupt. The dispersed individual is defined as a universal attached to a specific person whose sharing is prevented in a collective sense but allowed in an alternative sense, unlike a non-dispersed particular whose unique essence prevents any sharing. Individuation (tashakhkhus) is described as a non-entity (amr 'adami), not an actual restriction on the universal. If one insists it is a restricted universal, the author responds that the restriction is by an individuation that is itself dispersed, which is incoherent. Further objections are raised: if the dispersed individual existed in all persons, it would be universal; if in some only, that would be arbitrary. Therefore, the truth is that it has no external existence; it is merely a mental construct. The author concludes that if their intent is simply a mental observation, it is acceptable, but otherwise it is baseless.

التشخص

الجزئي

The Famous Doubt on Universal and Particular Definitions
Author presents the famous doubt and refutes it through the concept of huwiyya mumtaza and Ibn Sina's distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the famous doubt concerning the identity of external and mental forms, and explain how the concept of huwiyya mumtaza resolves it.

Core Idea
The doubt mistakes the external object for its mental image; the refutation shows they share a distinct identity that is not multiplied.

The definitions of universal and particular are foundational in logic. But a classical doubt arises: if external objects are present in the mind exactly as they are, then the same form becomes multiple in different minds, thereby violating the definitions. How do logicians answer? This lesson unpacks the doubt and the refutation presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the doubt
The external form of Zayd and the forms in multiple minds are considered identical. That means the one external form is replicated in each mind, threatening the definitions of universal and particular.
The text says 'fantaqada ta'rif al-kulli man'an wa ta'rif al-juz'i jam'an' – the definitions are violated in terms of prevention and inclusion.
2
Consider the three theories of vision
The author mentions three doctrines (emission, presence, acquisition) to contextualize how the mental image relates to the external object. This shows the doubt is not trivial.
The Peripatetic view (image as phantom) might seem to support the doubt, but is later dismissed.
3
Focus on the refutation: huwiyya mumtaza
The key is the concept of 'huwiyya mumtaza' – a distinct identity that underlies particularity. This identity is common between sensory images and the external object, but it is not a phantom. Its relation to images is like the universal to its individuals.
Because this identity is subject to material accidents, it cannot enter the intellect, only the senses. Also, it contains a unique distinguishing identity, so it cannot be multiplied externally.
4
Apply Ibn Sina's distinction
Ibn Sina distinguishes sensible, imaginary, and intelligible. The same Zayd can be sensible (with accidents), imaginary (without presence), and intelligible (abstract essence). This explains why the identity is not literally transferred.
When Zayd is sensible, he has accidents like 'where' and 'posture' that can be removed without affecting the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ (١) ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الصُّورَةَ الْخَارِجِيَّةَ لِزَيْدٍ، وَالصُّورَةَ الْحَاصِلَةَ مِنْهُ فِي أَذْهَانِ طَائِفَةٍ)، أي في حواسهم (تَصَوَّرُوهَا كُلُّهَا مُتَصَادِقَةٌ) ؛ إذ تلك الصور بعينها الصور الخارجية؛ فَإِنَّ التَّحْقِيقَ أَنَّ حُصُولَ الْأَشْيَاءِ بأَنْفُسِهَا في الدُّهْن)، جزئية كانت أو كلية لَا بِأَشْبَاحِهَا وَأَمْثَالِهَا ، فَتِلْكَ الصُّورَةُ مُتَكَثَّرَةٌ (٢) على سبيل الاجتماع، فانتقض تعريف الكلي منعا، وتعريف الجزئي جمعا .
فصل في المعاني المفردة
لعلک تقول: إن مذاهب الحكماء في الإبصار ثلاثة: الأول بخروج الشعاء من البصر، وإليه مال الرياضيون، والثاني أنه إدراك حضوري، وحضور المبصر عند مقابلة الحاسة، كما في الإدراك، وإليه مال الإشراقية، وهو الأشبه، والثالث أنه بحصول الصور، وإليه مال المشاؤون، وقد صرح الشيخ في كتاب النفس من طبيعيات «الشفاء»: أن الصورة شبح للمبصر، فالإشكال يتوجه على مذاهب أصلا، وكيف يجوز عاقل حصول شيء كبير في محل صغير.
نعم، بعض عبارات «شرح المواقف توهم ذلك، فهي مؤولة أو مردودة.
فاعلم: أنك قد عرفت سابقا أن المعروض للجزئية هي الهوية الممتازة عن الأشخاص الأخر العينية المشتركة بين الصور الحسية وصاحبها، وليست تلك الهوية شبحا بل نسبتها إلى الصور الحاصلة في الحواس نسبة الكلي إلى أشخاصه، لكن لمعروضيتها للعوارض المادية لا يمكن حصولها في العقل، بل في الحواس فقط، ولاشتمالها على هوية بها امتازت عن الهويات العينية الأخر لا يجوز العقل تكثره في الأشخاص الخارجية، وعلى هذا فالإشكال متوجه توجها ظاهرا.
قال الشيخ في «الإشارات»: الشيء قد يكون محسوسا عند ما يشاهد، ثم يكون متخيلا عند غيبته بتمثل صورته في الباطن، كزيد الذي أبصرته إذا غاب عنك فتخيلته، وقد يكون معقولا عند ما يتصور من زيد مثلا معنى الإنسان الموجود لغيره وهو عند ما يكون محسوسا قد غشيه غواش غريبة من ماهيته، لو أزيلت عنه لم تؤثر في كنه ماهيته، مثل أين ووضع وكيف ومقدار.
And here is a famous doubt, and it is that the external form of Zayd, and the form obtained from him in the minds of a group, that is in their senses, they all conceive them as coinciding; because those forms are exactly the external forms; for the verification is that objects are obtained by themselves in the mind, whether particular or universal, not by their phantoms and likenesses, so that form becomes multiple collectively, so the definition of the universal is violated in terms of prevention (man'), and the definition of the particular is violated in terms of inclusion (jam'). Section on simple meanings. Perhaps you will say: The doctrines of the philosophers concerning vision are three: first, by the emission of rays from the eye, adopted by the mathematicians; second, that it is a presentational perception and the presence of the visible object when facing the sense, as in perception, adopted by the Illuminationists, and it is the more likely; third, that it is by the acquisition of images, adopted by the Peripatetics, and the Shaykh has stated in the Book of the Soul from the Physics of al-Shifa that the image is a phantom of the visible object, so the objection applies to the doctrines fundamentally, and how can a rational person accept the obtaining of a large thing in a small place. Yes, some expressions from Sharh al-Mawaqif suggest that, so they are either interpretable or rejected. Know that you have already known earlier that that which underlies particularity is the identity distinguished from other external persons, common between the sensory images and their owner, and that identity is not a phantom, rather its relation to the images obtained in the senses is the relation of the universal to its individuals, but because it is subject to material accidents, it cannot be obtained in the intellect, but only in the senses, and because it includes an identity by which it is distinguished from other external identities, the intellect does not permit its multiplication in external persons, and on this basis, the objection stands manifestly. The Shaykh said in al-Isharat: A thing may be sensible when it is observed, then imagined when it is absent by the representation of its image in the inner sense, like Zayd whom you saw, when he is absent from you and you imagine him, and it may be intelligible when one conceives from Zayd, for example, the meaning of human existing for others, and when it is sensible, it is covered by strange accidents not of its essence, which if removed, do not affect the core of its essence, such as where, posture, quality, and quantity.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents a famous doubt (shakk mashhur) regarding the definitions of universal and particular, followed by a refutation. The doubt is based on the claim that the external form of Zayd and the mental forms obtained by multiple observers are identical (mutasadiqa). If objects are present in the mind by themselves (bi-anfusiha), not by phantoms, then the one external form becomes multiple in different minds collectively. This would violate the definition of universal (which prevents multiple instantiations in one subject? Actually, the text says 'fantaqada ta'rif al-kulli man'an' and 'ta'rif al-juz'i jam'an' — the universal definition requires it to be applicable to many, but here the form is one yet many, so 'man' would fail? Or 'jam' would fail? The phrasing 'man'an' and 'jam'an' likely refer to the two parts of the definition of universal: that it prevents (man') being restricted to one, and that it includes (jam') many. Here the external form is one but appears many in minds, so the preventive aspect is contradicted for the particular? It is a complex issue.) The author then begins a section on simple meanings (fasl fi al-ma'ani al-mufradah), where he outlines three doctrines of vision (emission, presence, acquisition). He notes that the peripatetic view (that the image is a phantom) might seem to support the doubt, but he dismisses expressions in Sharh al-Mawaqif as interpretable or rejected. The refutation proper starts with 'fa'lam': you already know that what underlies particularity is the identity (huwiyya) distinct from others, shared between sensory images and the external object. This identity is not a phantom; its relation to images is like the universal to its individuals. But because it is subject to material accidents, it cannot enter the intellect, only the senses. Also, because it includes a unique distinct identity, it cannot be multiplied externally. Thus the doubt is apparent but resolved. The passage ends with a quotation from Ibn Sina's al-Isharat distinguishing sensible, imaginary, and intelligible: the sensible object is covered by accidental features (where, posture, etc.), which if removed, the essence remains. This supports the idea that the identity of Zayd is not transferred in its material accidents.

The Real Particular as Predicate and Universal as Shadow
Author defends predication of the real particular and refines the shadow metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and defend the position that the real particular can be a predicate using the shadow metaphor and co-extensiveness argument.

Core Idea
The real particular is predicable, and the universal is but a shadow cast by particulars.

This passage tackles a critical dispute in Islamic logic: can a real particular, like the individual Zaid, be predicated of something? The author argues yes, contra al-Sayyid al-Jurjani, and offers a sophisticated defense using the metaphor of shadow. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of predication and abstraction, which are essential for analyzing propositions in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis
Locate the author's statement that the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) can be a predicate (mahmul). The phrase is: 'كون الجزئي الحقيقي... محمولا'.
2
Recall the opponent's view
Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani claimed the particular is never predicated. How does the author reinterpret al-Sayyid's claim? He suggests al-Sayyid may only mean an individual person is not predicated of another similar person.
3
Examine the shadow argument
The author analyzes al-Sayyid's definition of the universal as a shadow (zill) of the many. Why does the author find this problematic if applied to the real particular? Because the external form (a particular) would then have a multiple shadow, but it should have only one shadow.
4
Understand the co-extensiveness solution
The author introduces al-tasadiq (co-extensiveness) between external and mental forms. How does this resolve the shadow issue? Since both forms apply to the same subject, each can be abstracted from the other, making the shadow mutual and consistent with predication.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'This specific act is wajib' in fiqh. The subject is a particular (this act), and the predicate 'wajib' is a universal. Does the author's view allow this predication? Yes, and the mental form of the act is co-extensive with its external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعاني المفردة
كون الجزئي الحقيقي، وهو الهوية المشتركة بين الشخص الخارجي والذهني محمولا، لا كما زعم السيد قدس سره: أن الجزئي لا يحمل على شيء أصلا، ولعل مراده قدس سره أن الشخص لا يحمل على مثله. سيجيئ فانتظره. وهو كون الجزئي محمولا الحق. ولا يجاب كما أجاب السيد المحقق قدس سره بأن تعريف الكلي صدقها على الكثيرين هو ظل لها ومنتزع عنها: فإن الكلي ينتزع عن الجزئيات، واللازم ههنا أن لها ظلا متعددا، لا أنها ظل متعدد، بل ظل واحد. والمطلب الثاني لم يتحقق في مورد النقض، وذلك لأن الصورة الخارجية والصورة الذهنية متصادقة، والتصادق يصحح الانتزاع، والظلية أيضا: فإن الموجب للانتزاع
Chapter on Simple Meanings
The real particular—which is the identity common between the external person and the mental person—being predicated, not as al-Sayyid (may God sanctify his secret) claimed: that the particular is not predicated of anything at all. Perhaps his intention (may God sanctify his secret) is that the person is not predicated of its like. It will come, so wait for it. And that the particular is predicated is the truth. And it is not answered as the investigator al-Sayyid (may God sanctify his secret) answered: that the definition of the universal, namely its being true of many, is a shadow of them and abstracted from them. For the universal is abstracted from the particulars, and the necessary consequence here is that it (i.e., the external form) has a multiple shadow, not that it is a multiple shadow, but rather one shadow. And the second demand has not been realized in the locus of the objection. That is because the external form and the mental form are co-extensive, and co-extensiveness validates abstraction, and also the shadow-ness: for that which necessitates abstraction
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative section where the author defends the position that the real particular (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) can be predicated (mahmul). The author opens a new chapter on simple meanings. He states that the real particular, which is the identity common between the external individual and the mental individual, can be predicated. He criticizes al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, who held that the particular is never predicated of anything. The author suggests that al-Sayyid might only mean that an individual person is not predicated of another similar person. The author affirms that the correct view is that the particular can be predicated (e.g., 'Zaid is this person'). He then addresses an objection: al-Sayyid defined the universal as 'being true of many' and described it as a shadow (zill) of the many, abstracted from them. The author argues that if the universal is a shadow of the many, then the external form (a particular) would have a multiple shadow, but in reality it has only one shadow (the universal as a single concept). The author then mentions a 'second demand' that has not been answered in the objection: the external form and mental form are co-extensive (mutasadiqa), meaning they apply to the same subject; this co-extensiveness validates the abstraction of one from the other, and the shadow-ness (zilliyya) is mutual. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is that because the mental form and external form correspond, each can be considered a shadow of the other, though the universal is the shadow of the many particulars.

Key Terms
الجزئي الحقيقي
الظل
التصادق

Checkpoint

A) The particular cannot be predicated.
B) The universal is a shadow of many.
C) Abstraction of one from the other and mutual shadow-ness.
D) Al-Sayyid al-Jurjani's view is correct.

Key Takeaway
The real particular can be predicated; the universal is a shadow of particulars, and co-extensiveness between external and mental forms explains how abstraction works.

The Particular Cannot Be a Definition or Defined
Author states that particulars cannot be definitions or be defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and relative particulars and explain why particulars cannot serve as definitions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Particulars are known by sensation, not by logical definition.

In this passage, the author states a fundamental principle: the particular cannot be a definer (kāsib) nor defined (muktasab). This is because particulars are directly perceived by the senses, not by logical analysis. The author then distinguishes two senses of 'particular': the real particular (al-juz'i al-ḥaqīqī), which is a unique individual, and the relative particular (al-juz'i al-iḍāfī), which is a concept narrower than a broader universal. This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first sentence: 'The particular is not a definer nor defined.' This is the core assertion.
2
Understand the reason
The author gives the reason: 'because sensation is sufficient for its perception.' Why does this exclude particulars? Because definitions provide abstract knowledge, not sensory recognition.
3
Distinguish the two types of particular
The second sentence clarifies two senses: real particular (a unique individual like Zayd) and relative particular (a concept subsumed under a broader universal, e.g., 'human' relative to 'animal').
The relative particular is still a universal in a different context; it is particular only relative to a higher universal.
4
Apply the concept to Islamic sciences
Consider how definitions in fiqh and kalam always target universals. For example, defining 'khamr' requires a universal covering all intoxicants, not a specific drink.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage explains why particulars are not the concern of the logician. The first sentence states that a particular (juz'i) cannot serve as a definition (kasib) nor can it be defined (muktasab) because sensation is enough to know it. In logic, definitions (hudud) are only for universals. The second sentence clarifies the two types of particular: the 'real particular' (al-juz'i al-haqiqi) which is a unique individual, and the 'relative particular' (al-juz'i al-idafi) which is a universal that is more specific than another universal. For example, 'human' is a particular relative to 'animal', but a universal relative to 'Zayd'. The logician deals with the relative particular in terms of classification.

The Four Relationships between Universals
Author enumerates the four possible relations between two universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any pair of universals into one of the four relations: equality, mutual exclusivity, overlapping generality, or absolute generality.

Core Idea
Coincidence and difference in extension define all logical relations between universals.

This passage classifies the four possible relations between any two universals. Since logic deals with universals, not particulars, this classification is essential for building definitions and arguments. You will learn to distinguish equality, mutual exclusivity, overlapping generality, and absolute generality based on the logical behavior of affirmative and negative propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Take any two universals, e.g., 'animal' and 'human'. Determine if they have full, partial, or no overlap in extension.
2
Check for full coincidence (tasāduq kullī)
If every individual of the first is also an individual of the second, and vice versa, they are equal (mutasāwiyān). Example: 'human' and 'rational animal'.
Full coincidence is indicated by two universal affirmative propositions.
3
Check for full difference (tabāyun)
If no individual of one is an individual of the other, they are mutually exclusive (mutabāyinān). Example: 'animal' and 'mineral'.
Full difference is indicated by two universal negative propositions.
4
Check for partial overlap from both sides
If they overlap partially, such that each has individuals not in the other, they are overlapping (aʿamm wa akhaṣṣ min wajh). Example: 'white' and 'animal'.
Indicated by a particular affirmative from one side and two particular negatives.
5
Check for partial overlap from one side
If one universal fully contains the other but not vice versa, they are absolute general and specific (aʿamm wa akhaṣṣ muṭlaqan). Example: 'animal' (general) and 'human' (specific).
Indicated by a universal affirmative from one side and a particular negative from the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَبْحَثُ : النِّسَبُ بَيْنَ الكَليَّاتِ ولما لم يكن للمنطقي شغل بالجزئيات خص البحث بالكليات، وشرع فيه فقال: (الْكُلِّيَّانِ إِنْ تَصَادَقَا كُليًّا بأن ينعقد منهما موجبتان كليتان حضر النسب مطلقتان عامتان ؛ ليدخل النائم والمستيقظ (فمتساويان، وإلا فتفارقا). أي في أربع افترقا بأن يصدق أحدهما بدون الآخر، فإن كان) الافتراق (كليا من الْجَانِبَيْن، بأن يصدق سالبتان كليتان دائمتان (فمتباينان، وإن كان) الافتراق ( جُزْئِيَّا ، فَإِمَّا مِن الْجَانِبَيْن، بأن يصدق موجبة جزئية مطلقة عامة وسالبتان جزئيتان دائمتان (فَأَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مِنْ وَجْهِ، أَوْ) كان الافتراق جزئيا (مِنْ جَانِبِ وَاحِدٍ فَقَط)، بأن يصدق موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة من جانب، وسالبة جزئية دائمة من الآخر (فَأَعَمُّ وَأَخَصُّ مُطْلَقًا) . وهذا الحصر منقوض بالكليات التي لا تصدق في نفس الأمر على موجود أو معدوم ، كمفهوم ضروري الطرفين وشريك الباري، والمفهوم الذي وجوده وعدمه مستلزم للمحال . والجواب بتخصيص الْمَقْسِم بما له صدق على الأفراد في نفس الأمر، وقد يجاب بالإرادة من التصادق والتفارق الغير البين.
Section: The Relationships between Universals. Since the logician has no concern with particulars, he restricts investigation to universals, and proceeds saying: 'The two universals, if they coincide as a total coincidence (i.e., from both sides), such that two universal affirmative propositions are formed, then the two relationships are two absolute general statements, to include the sleeping and the awake, then they are equal. Otherwise, they differ.' That is, they differ in four ways: one may be true without the other. If the difference is total from both sides, such that two universal negative propositions are always true, then they are mutually exclusive. If the difference is partial, then either from both sides, such that a particular affirmative absolute general and two particular negative permanent are true, then they are overlapping (lit. more general and more specific from one aspect). Or if the difference is partial from one side only, such that a universal affirmative absolute general is true from one side, and a particular negative permanent from the other, then they are absolute generality and specificity. This enumeration is refuted by universals that have no actual existence in reality, like the concept of the necessary of both ends and the partner of the Creator, and the concept whose existence and non-existence both entail impossibility. The answer is to restrict the division to what has actual individuals, or it may be answered by intending non-evident coincidence and difference.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage classifies the logical relationships between any two universals. Since logic deals with universals, not particulars, the author explains the four relations. First, if two universals have the same extension (e.g., 'human' and 'rational animal'), they are equal (mutasāwiyān). Second, if they have no overlap (e.g., 'animal' and 'mineral'), they are mutually exclusive (mutabāyinān). Third, if they intersect (e.g., 'animal' and 'white'), one is more general in one aspect, more specific in another (aʿamm wa akhaṣṣ min wajh). Fourth, if one fully includes the other (e.g., 'animal' and 'human'), they are absolute general and specific (aʿamm wa akhaṣṣ muṭlaqan). The passage then notes a criticism: some concepts like 'partner of God' (sharīk al-bārī) have no actual instances, so they break the enumeration. The author replies by restricting the classification to concepts that have actual individuals.

التفارق

أعم وأخص من وجه
أعم وأخص مطلقا

Key Takeaway
The four relations—equality, exclusivity, overlapping, and absolute—are determined by the patterns of coincidence and difference in extension.

The Exception of Empty Universals in the Fourfold Classification
Criticism and response regarding empty universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify empty universals (those with no actual individuals) and explain why they are excluded from the fourfold classification.

Core Idea
Empty universals break the fourfold pattern; only universals with actual individuals are classified.

The passage notes a criticism: some universals like 'partner of God' have no actual instances, so they do not fit into the four relations. The author responds by restricting the classification to universals that have actual individuals. This lesson explores why this matters and how to handle such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criticism
The fourfold division is said to be refuted by universals that have no actual existence, like 'partner of God' (sharīk al-bārī). These concepts have no individuals, so coincidence or difference cannot be determined.
2
Understand the problem
For such universals, there are no individuals to check, so they cannot be placed in any of the four categories. This is a logical problem if the division claims to be exhaustive.
3
Examine the author's first response
The author restricts the division to universals that have actual individuals (mā lahu ṣidq ʿalā al-afrād fī nafs al-amr). Only consider universals that apply to at least one existing thing.
4
Consider the alternative response
The author also mentions interpreting 'coincidence' and 'difference' as non-evident (ghayr bayyin), meaning the classification might refer to logical relationships between concepts rather than actual extension. However, the first response is standard.
5
Apply the refinement
When encountering a universal, first check if it has any actual individuals. If it does not, it is excluded from the fourfold classification. This ensures the classification remains valid.

The Four Relations Between Universal Concepts
Author enumerates four types of relations between universals: equality, divergence, general-specific from one aspect, and absolute general-specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relations (equality, divergence, partial overlap, or inclusion) holds between any two universal concepts.

Core Idea
Every pair of universal concepts stands in one of four logical relations: equality, divergence, overlap, or inclusion.

When scholars of logic discuss definitions or arguments, they need to know how two universal concepts relate. This passage from the Bahr al-‘Ulum lays out the four possible relations: equality, divergence, partial overlap (general-specific from one aspect), and full inclusion (absolute general-specific). Mastering these relations allows you to evaluate definitions and recognize the logical structure of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Check for coextension (tasāwī)
Determine if the two concepts have exactly the same instances. If every A is B and every B is A, they are equal.
2
Check for complete separation (tabāyun)
If no individual is both A and B, they are divergent. For example, 'human' and 'stone' have no overlap.
3
Check for partial overlap (umūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh)
If they share some instances but each also has instances the other lacks, they overlap from one aspect.
4
Check for inclusion (umūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq)
If all instances of one are instances of the other, but not vice versa, then one is absolutely general and the other absolutely specific.
5
Apply the test using propositional truth
Use the truth conditions: equality requires two universal affirmatives; divergence requires two universal negatives; partial overlap requires one particular affirmative and two particular negatives; inclusion requires one universal affirmative and one particular negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حضر النسب مطلقتان عامتان ; ليدخل النائم والمستيقظ (فمتساويان، وإلا فتفارقا). أي في أربع افترقا بأن يصدق أحدهما بدون الآخر، فإن كان الافتراق كليا من الجانبين، بأن يصدق سالبتان كليتان دائمتان (فمتباينان، وإن كان الافتراق جزئيا، فإما من الجانبين)، بأن يصدق موجبة جزئية مطلقة عامة وسالبتان جزئيتان دائمتان (فأعم وأخص من وجه، أو كان الافتراق جزئيا من جانب واحد فقط)، بأن يصدق موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة من جانب، وسالبة جزئية دائمة من الآخر (فأعم وأخص مطلقا).
The presence of relations [Or: enumeration of relations] are two absolute generals; to include the sleeper and the awakened (so they are equal, otherwise they differ). That is, they differ in four ways by one being true without the other. If the difference is total from both sides, such that two universal negatives are always true, then they are divergent. If the difference is partial, then either from both sides, such that a particular affirmative is absolutely general and two particular negatives are always true, then they are general and specific from one aspect; or the difference is partial from one side only, such that a universal affirmative is absolutely general from one side and a particular negative is always true from the other, then they are absolute general and specific.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines the four classical relations between universal concepts in logic. The author begins by stating that the relations are of two absolute generals—possibly meaning that the initial pair are both universal quantifiers? The example of the sleeper and the awakened illustrates equality: the concept 'sleeper' and 'awakened' are equal in extension (every sleeper is not-awakened, but here it may mean that the two terms are coextensive in a specific context). The key point is that when two concepts are not equal, they differ in one of four ways based on the truth conditions of propositions. The first type is complete divergence (tabāyun): two concepts are divergent if all universal negatives are always true between them, meaning no individual has both properties. For example, 'human' and 'horse' are divergent: 'No human is a horse' is always true. The second type is general-specific from one aspect (umūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh): they share some individuals and each has some individuals the other does not. This is indicated by a true particular affirmative (some A are B) and two particular negatives (some A are not B and some B are not A) being always true? The text says 'two particular negatives always true', which is a bit unclear; standard definition requires that the two concepts are not coextensive and not completely separate, but have partial overlap. The third type is absolute general-specific (umūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq): one concept is entirely within the other but not vice versa. The condition is a universal affirmative from one side (all A are B) and a particular negative from the other (some B are not A) always true. For example, 'animal' and 'human': all humans are animals, but some animals are not humans. This classification is foundational for evaluating definitions and arguments.

Key Terms
متساويان
متباينان
أعم وأخص من وجه
أعم وأخص مطلقا
تصادق

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Divergence (tabāyun)

Key Takeaway
The four relations — equality, divergence, partial overlap, and inclusion — are determined by the truth conditions of categorical propositions between the two concepts.

The Problem of Empty Concepts in the Fourfold Classification
Objection that the fourfold classification fails for concepts with no actual instances, and two replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify concepts with no actual instances and explain how the classification of logical relations is restricted to address them.

Core Idea
Empty concepts break the fourfold classification; the division must restrict to concepts with actual instances.

We have learned that the five universals can be compared in terms of equality, co-extensiveness, and so on. But what happens when a concept has no actual individuals at all? This passage raises an important objection: such empty concepts cannot be placed into the fourfold relation scheme because they have no extension. The author then offers two remedies: restrict the domain or reinterpret the terms. This lesson will walk through the objection and the responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The passage states that the enumeration (of the four relations) is refuted by universals that are not true of any existent or non-existent. Understand that the classification assumes concepts have actual instances, which is not always true.
The objection is that the classification is incomplete or invalid for empty concepts.
2
Recognize the examples
Three examples are given: (1) 'necessary-of-both-sides' – a logically impossible concept (e.g., a square circle), (2) 'partner of God' – a theologically impossible concept, and (3) a concept whose existence and non-existence both entail impossibility. These have no instances in reality.
These are extreme cases, but the logical point applies to any concept with zero extension.
3
Understand the first reply
The first answer is to restrict the 'divided thing' (al-maqsam) to concepts that have truth about individuals in external reality. That is, the classification only applies to concepts with actual instances.
This solves the problem by excluding empty concepts from the domain.
4
Understand the second reply
The second answer is to intend by 'co-truthfulness' and 'difference' a non-evident (ghayr bayyin) sense. This may mean the relations are interpreted purely logically, without requiring existence, but the text does not elaborate.
This preserves the classification for all concepts but weakens the existential commitment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الحصر منقوض بالكليات التي لا تصدق في نفس الأمر على موجود أو معدوم ، كمفهوم ضروري الطرفين وشريك الباري، والمفهوم الذي وجوده وعدمه مستلزم للمحال . والجواب بتخصيص المقسم بما له صدق على الأفراد في نفس الأمر، وقد يجاب بالإرادة من التصادق والتفارق الغير البين.
And this enumeration is refuted by the universals that are not true in reality of an existent or non-existent, such as the concept of necessary-of-both-sides and the partner of God, and the concept whose existence and non-existence implies an impossibility. The answer is by restricting the division to that which has truth about individuals in reality, and it may be answered by intending co-truthfulness and difference that is not evident.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the previous classification of relations, and then offers two responses. The objection is that the four relations (equality, etc.) assume that the concepts have actual instances. But some concepts have no actual instances at all—either because they are logically impossible (like 'necessary from both sides'—a concept that cannot be applied to anything) or religiously impossible (like 'partner of God', which cannot exist), or because both their existence and non-existence entail a contradiction. For such concepts, it is meaningless to speak of co-extension or difference. The first answer is to restrict the division (the 'divided thing'—al-maqsam) to concepts that actually apply to individuals in external reality. The second answer is that the division can be interpreted in terms of 'clear' (bayyin) co-truthfulness and difference—meaning that the relations are understood in a logical sense even if the concepts are empty, but this is not elaborated here. This discussion shows the need for clear existential import in logical relations.

Key Terms
ضروري الطرفين
شريك الباري

D) Because they are negative concepts

Key Takeaway
Logical classifications of relations must be restricted to concepts that actually have instances in reality; empty concepts require special handling.
